Induction and Analogy

Deduction and Induction: Core Intellectual Operations
Author enumerates eight types of logical operations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between deduction (deriving particulars from universals) and induction (deriving universals from particulars) using Islamic scientific examples.

Core Idea
Deduction applies universals to particulars; induction builds universals from particulars.

The author begins his list with two complementary operations: deriving particulars from universals (deduction) and deriving universals from examining particulars (induction). These are foundational to all Islamic sciences, from fiqh to kalam, as they enable both application of general rules and formation of general principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the direction of reasoning
For deduction: you move from a general rule to a specific case. For induction: you move from multiple specific cases to a general rule.
This is the key difference.
2
Recall a familiar Islamic example
Deduction: 'Every intoxicant is haram. This drink is an intoxicant, so it is haram.' Induction: 'I observed that dates, grapes, and barley all produce intoxication when fermented; therefore, all fermented drinks are likely intoxicating.'
Induction gives probability unless complete enumeration.
3
Check the certainty level
Deductive conclusions are certain if premises are true; inductive conclusions are probabilistic (zanni) unless complete enumeration occurs.
The author mentions induction as a valid method even without logical necessity.
4
Distinguish from other operations
Note that the author also lists generalization through 'self-evident laws' (qawanin dhatiyya) alongside induction.
This highlights the epistemic diversity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبما يجتمع لدى العقل من صور المدركات عن طريق الحس الظاهر أو الباطن، أو عن طريق أخبار الآخرين التي تحكي ما توصلوا إليه من معارف، وبما يتخيله من صور تركيبية جديدة يبتكرها، وبما لديه من أصول فطرية تتألف منها موازينه وقوانينه الذاتية، يستطيع أن يقوم بأعمال فكرية كثيرة، منها الأعمال التالية : ١ - يستطيع العقل بنفسه استنباط الجزئيات من الكليات، وإدراك الكليات من النظر في الجزئيات، وتعميم الأحكام عن طريق قوانينه الذاتية، أو عن طريق الاستقراء . ٢ - ويستطيع العقل قياس الأشباه والنظائر بعضها على بعض . ٣ - ويستطيع العقل استنباط مقابلات المعاني وعكوسها، فيدرك النقيض وما هو داخل فيه متى عرف النقيض، ويدرك العكس متى عرف الأصل . ٤ - ويستطيع العقل بنفسه أن يجري أعمال التحليل والتركيب والجمع والتفريق فيما لديه من مدركات . ٥ - ويستطيع العقل بنفسه أن يدرك النسب بين المعاني والمدركات التي لديه . ٦ - ويستطيع العقل بنفسه إدراك الروابط بين المعلولات وعللها العقلية، وبين المسببات وأسبابها المنطقية، وأن يدرك آثار الأشياء ونتائجها المنطقية المستندة إلى مبدأ العلة العقلية، أو السبب المنطقي . ٧ - ويستطيع العقل أن يدرك من ملاحظته للتجارب المتكررة أحكاماً عامة، ولو لم يكن بين السبب والظاهرة ارتباط عقلي . ٨ - ومتى عرف العقل الشيء الناقص، فإنه يستطيع بنفسه أن ينطلق في
And by what comes together in the mind of forms of perceptions through the external or internal sense, or through reports of others that convey what they have arrived at of knowledge, and by what it imagines of new composite forms it invents, and by what it has of innate principles from which its measures and self-evident laws are composed, it can perform many intellectual works, including the following: 1- The mind can by itself deduce particulars from universals, and perceive universals from examining particulars, and generalize judgments through its self-evident laws or through induction. 2- And the mind can analogize similar things and parallels to each other. 3- And the mind can deduce the opposites of meanings and their inverses, so it perceives the contradictory and what is included in it once it knows the contradictory, and it perceives the inverse once it knows the original. 4- And the mind can by itself carry out operations of analysis, synthesis, combination, and separation among the perceptions it has. 5- And the mind can by itself perceive the relations between the meanings and perceptions it has. 6- And the mind can by itself perceive the connections between effects and their intellectual causes, and between caused things and their logical reasons, and it can perceive the logical effects and consequences of things based on the principle of intellectual causality or logical reason. 7- And the mind can perceive from its observation of repeated experiments general judgments, even if there is no intellectual connection between the cause and the phenomenon. 8- And when the mind knows an incomplete thing, it can by itself launch into...
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the intellectual operations the mind can perform. The author first states the raw materials: perceptions from senses, reports from others, invented imaginative forms, and innate principles. Then he lists eight operations. (1) Deduction and induction: deriving particulars from universals (like applying a general rule to a specific case) and deriving universals from particulars (like inducing a general rule from multiple instances). (2) Analogy (qiyas): comparing similar things, e.g., comparing nabidh to wine in terms of prohibition due to intoxication. (3) Deriving opposites and inverses: once you know the contradictory of something, you know its opposite; once you know the original, you know its inverse (e.g., from 'every human is mortal' you know 'some mortal is human' - the converse). (4) Analysis and synthesis: breaking down concepts and combining them, e.g., analyzing the definition of prayer into its components. (5) Perceiving relations: e.g., perceiving the relation of fatherhood. (6) Perceiving causal connections: between effects and causes, e.g., knowing that fire causes burning based on the principle of causality. (7) Generalization from repeated experiments: even without a necessary logical connection, like nicotine causing cancer based on repeated observation. (8) Extending from the incomplete: if the mind knows a partial thing, it can extend to the complete, e.g., knowing one attribute of Allah leads to understanding others through inference. The author emphasizes that these operations are performed by the mind itself, using its innate laws.

Key Terms
استنباط الجزئيات من الكليات
قياس الأشباه والنظائر
الاستقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which operation involves deriving particular instances from universal rules?
A) Induction
B) Deduction
C) Analogy
D) Analysis

Key Takeaway
Deduction and induction are two foundational intellectual operations that move between universals and particulars, but with different directions of reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Provide one example of deduction from Islamic law (using any general principle) and one example of induction from the Islamic tradition (e.g., deriving a general ruling from multiple similar cases).

Induction and Analogy

Analogy and Derivation of Opposites
Author enumerates eight types of logical operations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify analogical reasoning (qiyas al-ashbah wa al-nazair) and the derivation of opposites and inverses, using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Analogy extends a ruling from one case to a similar case; knowing opposites and inverses expands knowledge without new observation.

After deduction and induction, the author lists two operations that extend knowledge laterally: analogy between similar things, and deriving opposites and inverses from known meanings. These are widely used in fiqh (analogy) and logic (conversion and contraposition).

Guided Path
1
Understand analogy
Analogy (qiyas) here means comparing two similar things to transfer a judgment from one to the other. In fiqh, this is the fourth source of law.
The author uses the phrase 'qiyas al-ashbah wa al-nazair'.
2
Example of analogy
Wine is haram due to intoxication. Nabidh (date wine) shares the intoxication property, so it is also haram.
This is a standard fiqh analogy.
3
Understand opposites and inverses
The passage mentions: knowing the contradictory (naqid) allows knowing its opposite; knowing the original (asl) allows knowing its inverse (aks). In logic, this includes conversion and contraposition.
For example, 'Every A is B' converts to 'Some B is A'.
4
Example of inversion
If 'Every human is mortal' is true, its converse 'Some mortal is human' is also true. Or the contradictory: 'Not every human is mortal' is false.
This is logical inference without new empirical input.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the intellectual operations the mind can perform. The author first states the raw materials: perceptions from senses, reports from others, invented imaginative forms, and innate principles. Then he lists eight operations. (1) Deduction and induction: deriving particulars from universals (like applying a general rule to a specific case) and deriving universals from particulars (like inducing a general rule from multiple instances). (2) Analogy (qiyas): comparing similar things, e.g., comparing nabidh to wine in terms of prohibition due to intoxication. (3) Deriving opposites and inverses: once you know the contradictory of something, you know its opposite; once you know the original, you know its inverse (e.g., from 'every human is mortal' you know 'some mortal is human' - the converse). (4) Analysis and synthesis: breaking down concepts and combining them, e.g., analyzing the definition of prayer into its components. (5) Perceiving relations: e.g., perceiving the relation of fatherhood. (6) Perceiving causal connections: between effects and causes, e.g., knowing that fire causes burning based on the principle of causality. (7) Generalization from repeated experiments: even without a necessary logical connection, like nicotine causing cancer based on repeated observation. (8) Extending from the incomplete: if the mind knows a partial thing, it can extend to the complete, e.g., knowing one attribute of Allah leads to understanding others through inference. Analysis, Synthesis, Relations, Causality, and Extrapolation
Author enumerates eight types of logical operations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify operations of analysis/synthesis, perceiving relations, causal connections, and extrapolating from incomplete knowledge in Islamic scientific reasoning.

Core Idea
The mind can break down concepts, relate them, see causal links, and infer the whole from a part.

The remaining operations show the mind's ability to deconstruct and reconstruct knowledge, perceive relationships, understand causality, and complete partial knowledge. These are essential for deeper understanding in all Islamic sciences, from defining terms to proving theological doctrines.

Guided Path
1
Analysis and synthesis
Analysis breaks a concept into its components (e.g., defining prayer by its pillars); synthesis combines concepts to form new ones.
The author mentions 'tahlil, tarkib, jam', tafriq'.
2
Perceiving relations
The mind sees relationships like fatherhood, causality, or entailment between ideas.
This is distinct from causal reasoning below.
3
Causal connections
The mind infers effects from causes (e.g., fire causes burning) and causes from effects (e.g., smoke indicates fire), based on the principle of intellectual causality.
The author mentions 'al-illah al-'aqliyya' and 'al-sabab al-mantiqi'.
4
Extrapolating from the incomplete
When the mind knows a part, it can infer the whole. For example, knowing one attribute of Allah can lead to understanding others.
This is item 8, which is cut off but interpreted as completing incomplete knowledge.

Source Text

Induction and Analogy
beginner
5 min
Definition and Nature of Induction
Author defines induction as a method of indirect inference with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key features of induction as an intellectual-sensory process that uses intentional experimentation.

Core Idea
Induction is active inquiry: questioning objects through repeated experiments.

In this passage, the author defines induction (الاستقراء) as a method of indirect inference that combines rational thought and sensory observation. Unlike passive observation, induction often involves deliberate experiments where we ask practical questions to things and record their responses. This lesson will unpack the definition and show how the examples illustrate the process.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classification
Find where the author places induction among methods of inference. The passage says it is 'among the methods of indirect inference.' This tells you that induction does not produce direct intuitive knowledge but requires premises.
2
Analyze the dual nature
Note the phrase 'intellectual and sensory process together.' Induction is not just sensory perception; it also involves reasoning. Ask yourself why both are needed.
3
Understand intentional experiment
Read how the author defines 'التجربة المقصودة': posing practical questions to things and observing their responses. Contrast this with unintentional experiences that happen in daily life.
4
Interpret the examples
Read the examples about matter and copper. Notice the metaphorical 'language of circumstance' (لسان الحال). What does the object's response teach us about the process of induction?
5
Generalize the method
From the examples, summarize the inductive process: repeat experiments under varied conditions, observe the outcomes, and derive a general answer from the object's actual behavior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستقراء
من طرق الاستدلال غير المباشر طريقة الاستقراء. والاستقراء عملية فكرية وحسية معاً، وقد تستخدم هذه العملية التجربة المقصودة إضافة إلى التجارب غير المقصودة التي تمر في حياة الإنسان.
والتجربة المقصودة هي بمثابة طرح الأسئلة العملية على الأشياء، لمعرفة مدى استجابتها، أو عدم استجابتها، أو مدى تأثرها بما طرح عليها، وملاحظة كل ذلك وتقييده، باعتباره نتائج قدمتها التجارب.
أمثلة :
أ - نقول للمادة مثلاً : هل أنت قابلة للتأكسد بالحموض؟ فتقول المادة بلسان الحال: إنني مسخرة بين يديكم بتسخير الله، فجربوني مرات عديدة في ظروف شتى، وأحوال مختلفة، تأخذوا الجواب الصحيح من واقع حالي.
ب - ونقول للنحاس مثلاً : هل أنت صالح لأن نصنع منك بندقية وسائر أسلحة الحرب؟ .
فيقول النحاس : أنا مسخر بين يديكم بتسخير الله، فجربوني وامتحنوني بصور شتى، وأشكال عدة، وظروف مختلفات، أعطكم جواب أسئلتكم عملياً.
Induction
Among the methods of indirect inference is the method of induction. Induction is an intellectual and sensory process together, and this process may use intentional experimentation in addition to unintentional experiences that occur in human life.
The intentional experiment is like posing practical questions to things, to know the extent of their response, or lack of response, or the extent of their being affected by what is posed upon them, and observing all that and recording it, as results that the experiments provide.
Examples:
A - We say to matter, for example: Are you susceptible to oxidation by acids? So matter says in the language of the state: I am subjugated to you by the subjugation of God, so try me many times in various conditions and different states, you will take the correct answer from my actual reality.
B - And we say to copper, for example: Are you suitable for us to make a rifle and other weapons of war from you?
So copper says: I am subjugated to you by the subjugation of God, so try me and test me in various forms, numerous shapes, and different conditions, I will give you the answer to your questions practically.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a definitional explanation of induction (الاستقراء) as a method of indirect inference. The author begins by stating that induction is one of the methods of indirect reasoning, then defines it as a process that is both intellectual and sensory. This means induction involves both rational analysis and observation. The author distinguishes between intentional experiments (التجربة المقصودة) and unintentional experiences that happen naturally in life. Intentional experimentation is described as posing practical questions to objects to see how they respond or are affected, then recording the results. Two examples are given using the metaphorical language of objects speaking in 'the language of the state' (لسان الحال) – a common Arabic idiom meaning 'the language of circumstance' or 'the evidence of the situation'. In the first example, we ask matter whether it is susceptible to oxidation by acids, and matter replies: 'I am subjugated to you by God's subjugation; test me repeatedly under various conditions to get the correct answer.' This illustrates the process of repeated experimentation to discover a general law. The second example asks copper whether it is suitable for making weapons, and copper similarly invites testing. Both examples emphasize that the object is subjugated to humans by God, and through repeated experimentation under diverse conditions, we obtain practical answers. This passage serves to clarify the definition of induction and demonstrate its practical application in gaining knowledge through observation and experiment.

Key Terms
الاستقراء
التجربة المقصودة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To replace all unintentional experiences.
B) To ask practical questions to things and observe their responses.

D) To derive metaphysical truths without observation.

Key Takeaway
Induction as defined here is an active, combined intellectual-sensory process that uses repeated intentional experiments to discover general truths about objects.

?

Degrees of Inductive Knowledge: Certainty to Rejection
Author categorizes inductive knowledge by epistemic strength

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any inductive conclusion into one of three epistemic categories: certain (affirmative or negative), preponderant or equal probability (doubt), or weak probability, based on its proximity to certainty.

Core Idea
Induction yields not only probability but also certainty—on both the affirmative and negative sides.

We often think of induction as a method that only gives probable results, but this passage from the classical logic text shows a more nuanced picture. The author demonstrates that inductive conclusions can range from absolute certainty to weak probability, and even certainty can appear in the form of definitive rejection. Understanding this spectrum is crucial for evaluating evidence in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh and kalam, where inductive reasoning about scriptural indications and empirical regularities must be weighed with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sources of variation in inductive knowledge
The passage states that inductive knowledge varies in strength (قوته). Note the factors that determine closeness to certainty: whether the evidence precludes the opposite, or whether the probability is equal, preponderant, or weak.
The author mentions three specific cases (ب, جـ, ٣) but later adds a more general spectrum.
2
Distinguish the three main categories
The author lists: B) an opinion equal in strength to its opposite (doubt), C) a weak probability leaning toward rejection, and 3) the definitively rejected (certain negation). Observe that the definitively rejected is a type of certainty, not just strong probability.
The numbering skips from جـ to ٣, indicating a clear break between probability and certainty.
3
Map the categories onto the spectrum of certainty
In the concluding summary, the author compares induction to other methods: it yields certain knowledge (yaqin), or probabilities that are preponderant (rajih), equal (musawi), or outweighed (marjuh). Arrange these along a line from certain affirmation to certain negation, with doubt in the middle.
The definitively rejected (مرجوح رفضاً) corresponds to certainty on the negative side.
4
Apply the classification to a real Islamic science example
Consider a jurist's inductive survey of hadiths on a ruling. If all hadiths support one view and disconfirm the opposite, the jurist attains certainty. If some contradict, he reaches preponderant probability. If evidence is balanced, doubt. If all hadiths contradict a claim, he is certain it is false (definitively rejected). This shows induction's full epistemic range.
This example illustrates how the classification operates in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتفاوت قرباً وبعداً من اليقين بحسب قوته . ب - وإما أن يكون ظناً مساوياً في القوة لما يخالفه، وعندئذ يقف الفكر منه موقف الشك من غير ترجيح . جـ - وإما أن يكون ظناً ضعيفاً، أقرب إلى ترجيح الرفض منه إلى ترجيح القبول، وهو يتفاوت قرباً وبعداً من الرفض النهائي بقدر نسبة ضعفه . ٣ - ثم يأتي المرفوض نهائياً، وهو الذي لا يقبل احتمال الرجعة إلى جانب الإثبات بحال من الأحوال، وعندئذ يكون من اليقين أيضاً، ومن الحقائق النهائية التي نتوصل إليها، ولكن في جانب الرفض لا في جانب القبول، أي : في جانب النفي لا في جانب الإثبات . فشأننا في الاستقراء كشأننا في سائر الطرق التي نتوصل بها إلى معارف يقينية، أو دون اليقينية، من ظنية راجحة على ما يخالفها في تصورنا المستند إلى الأدلة، أو متساوية مع ما يخالفها، أو مرجوحة بالنسبة إليه .
It varies in closeness and distance from certainty according to its strength. B - Either it is a probability equal in strength to its opposite, at which point thought takes the position of doubt without preference. C - Or it is a weak probability, closer to preferring rejection than acceptance, and it varies in closeness and distance from final rejection according to the proportion of its weakness. 3 - Then comes the definitively rejected, which does not accept the possibility of returning to the side of affirmation under any circumstances, and at that point it is also certainty, and among the final truths we reach, but on the side of rejection, not acceptance, i.e., on the side of negation, not affirmation. So our situation with induction is like our situation with all other methods by which we attain certain knowledge, or below certainty: probable outweighing its opposite in our conception based on evidence, or equal to its opposite, or outweighed relative to it.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of the results of inductive inquiry according to their epistemic strength. The author begins by stating that inductive knowledge varies in proximity to certainty (yaqin). He then enumerates three distinct cases. First (B), an opinion that is equal in strength to its opposite—this is genuine doubt (shakk) where the mind finds no basis for preference. Second (C), a weak probability that leans more toward rejection than acceptance; its distance from final rejection is proportional to its weakness. Third (3), the definitively rejected, which is certain but on the negative side—like a certain falsehood. The author then summarizes: induction yields results on a spectrum from certainty to degrees of probability (rajih, musawi, marjuh) analogous to other epistemological methods. The classification is important because it shows that induction is not limited to producing mere likelihoods; it can also yield certainty in cases of conclusive counterevidence.

Key Terms
اليقين
الظن
الاستقراء
الشك
الراجح
المساوي
المرجوح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Certainty
B) Weak probability
C) Doubt
D) Definitively rejected

Key Takeaway
Inductive knowledge ranges from certainty (both affirmation and negation) through degrees of probability to doubt, and understanding this spectrum is vital for proper reasoning in Islamic sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following inductive conclusions according to the author's threefold scheme: (a) A scholar surveys all known hadiths on a topic and finds 20 supporting and none opposing. (b) A scholar finds equal numbers of supporting and opposing hadiths. (c) A scholar finds 5 hadiths supporting and 15 opposing, but the opposing hadiths are from weaker narrators. (d) A scholar finds that every single hadith contradicts a certain claim, and no counterevidence exists. Which categories correspond?
?
Using the author's classification, evaluate the strength of the inductive claim: 'The consensus of the Companions indicates that this ruling is established, but a few isolated reports suggest otherwise.' Where does this fall on the spectrum of certainty? Justify your answer.

Induction in Arabic Linguistics
Author shows induction in Arabic grammar extraction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct how Muslim grammarians used induction to derive rules of Arabic grammar and morphology.

Core Idea
Induction turned observed Arabic speech into grammatical rules.

This lesson explores how early grammarians surveyed vast corpora of Arabic to extract the rules of nahw and sarf. The passage highlights that this required strenuous effort but yielded systematic principles.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the inductive basis of grammar
Understand that grammarians did not invent rules a priori; they collected samples of actual Arabic speech from poetry, prose, and the Quran.
The passage says 'استقصوا فيها معظم التراكيب العربية' (they surveyed most Arabic constructions).
2
Analyze the method of survey
Notice that they covered vocabulary, derivatives, and syntactic patterns. They then generalized from these particulars.
This is the core inductive process: from many examples to a rule.
3
See the result: rules from data
The outcome was the disciplines of nahw (syntax) and sarf (morphology). These were derived inductively.
The passage calls these 'علوم التي توصل المسلمون إلى جلّ ما فيها عن طريق الاستقراء'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلمون وطريق الاستقراء : وكان الاستقراء هو الوسيلة التي اعتمد عليها علماء المسلمين حين استخرجوا قواعد اللغة العربية وضوابطها، وقد كلفهم ذلك جهوداً مضنية، إذ استقصوا فيها معظم التراكيب العربية وطرق أدائها، ومعظم المفردات العربية ومشتقاتها، ثم استخرجوا من ذلك ضوابط النحو والصرف، فكانت هذه من العلوم التي توصل المسلمون إلى جلّ ما فيها عن طريق الاستقراء . وكان للاستقراء دور مهم في استخراج علم العروض الذي وضعه الخليل، وفي ضبط زحافاته وعلله، إذ تتبع الشعر العربي، وأحصى وضبط ما شاهد فيه . وكان الاستقراء أحد الوسائل التي اعتمد عليها فقهاء المسلمين في طائفة من أبواب الفقه الإسلامي . فبالاستقراء استخرجوا القواعد الفقهية العامة، وبالاستقراء أحصوا المياه ثم ضبطوا أحكامها الشرعية، وعليه اعتمدوا في تحديد دماء الحيض والنفاس والاستحاضة، وفي تحديد مقدار مكث الأجنة في الأرحام، ثم استنبطوا أحكامها الشرعية. وعلى الاستقراء اعتمدوا في جمع أعمال الناس الشخصية وغير الشخصية، ثم اجتهدوا في استخراج الحكم الشرعي لكل عمل منها. وحين يبحث باحثهم في النصوص فإنه يعتمد أولاً على الاستقراء لجمع النصوص التي تتعلق ببحثه، ثم ينظر فيما جمعه منها، ويجتهد في فهمها وفق طرائق الاجتهاد التي تُحدّدها له أصول الفهم والاستنباط. أما الاستقراء لمجرد جمع المعارف فقد كان وسيلتهم في الحديث الشريف، وفي التاريخ والجغرافيا، وفي الأدب والحِكَم والأمثال والأخلاق والآداب وتدوين معارف الأولين، وغير ذلك. وكان الاستقراء المقرون بالتجارب العملية وسيلتهم في الكيمياء وفي الطب، ضمن حدود الأدوات التي كانت متيسرة لهم في أزمانهم. من كل هذا نلاحظ أن الاستقراء وسيلة كبرى من الوسائل التي اعتمد عليها علماء المسلمين، فيما توصلوا إليه من معارف، وفيما دونوه من علوم. ومع تفجر الثورة العلمية المادية الحديثة، أخذ الاستقراء دوراً أكبر في العالم الغربي، وتحدّدت له مفاهيم أكثر دقة وأكثر ضبطاً من الناحية العملية، وكان له في مجال العلوم المادية واستخراج قوانينها المقام الأول، وطبيعي أن يكون له هذا المقام العظيم، لأنه هو الوسيلة العملية النافعة التي يستطيع الإنسان بها التوصل إلى معرفة خصائص الكون، وصفات الأشياء، وسنن الله الثابتة في العالم الطبيعي.
Muslims and the method of induction: Induction was the means that Muslim scholars relied on when they extracted the rules and principles of the Arabic language. It cost them strenuous efforts, as they surveyed most Arabic constructions and methods of expression, and most Arabic vocabulary and derivatives, then extracted from that the rules of grammar and morphology. These were among the sciences which Muslims reached most of their content through induction. Induction had an important role in developing the science of prosody (al-‘arud) established by al-Khalil, and in regulating its deviations (zihafat) and defects (‘ilal), as he tracked Arabic poetry, enumerated and recorded what he observed in it. Induction was one of the methods upon which Muslim jurists relied in a number of areas of Islamic jurisprudence. Through induction they extracted general juristic rules; through induction they enumerated types of water and then regulated their legal rulings; upon it they relied in determining the blood of menstruation, postpartum bleeding, and istihada, and in determining the duration of the stay of fetuses in the wombs, and then deduced their legal rulings. Upon induction they relied in collecting people's personal and impersonal actions, then exercised ijtihad to extract the legal ruling for each action. When a researcher examines texts, he first relies on induction to collect the texts relevant to his research, then examines what he has collected, and exerts ijtihad in understanding them according to the methods of ijtihad defined for him by the principles of understanding and derivation. As for induction for the mere collection of knowledge, it was their method in hadith, in history and geography, in literature, wisdom, proverbs, ethics, good manners, recording the knowledge of the ancients, and other fields. Induction combined with practical experiments was their method in chemistry and medicine, within the limits of the tools available to them in their times. From all this we observe that induction is a major tool among the means upon which Muslim scholars relied in the knowledge they attained and the sciences they recorded. With the eruption of the modern material scientific revolution, induction took a greater role in the Western world, and its concepts became more precise and more rigorous practically, and it occupied the foremost position in the field of material sciences and the extraction of their laws. Naturally it has this great position, because it is the practical useful method by which man can attain knowledge of the properties of the universe, the qualities of things, and the steadfast laws of Allah in the natural world.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a historical-epistemological exposition of the role of induction (istiqra’) in Islamic civilization. The author begins by stating that Muslim scholars relied on induction to extract the rules of Arabic grammar and morphology, surveying a vast corpus of speech. He then mentions al-Khalil’s derivation of ‘arud (prosody) by inductively examining Arabic poetry. Next, he turns to fiqh: jurists used induction to formulate general legal principles (qawa‘id fiqhiyya), classify types of water and their rulings, determine menstrual and fetal periods, and collect actions for ijtihad. In textual research, induction is the first step for gathering relevant texts before interpretation. The author extends the method to hadith, history, geography, literature, ethics, and the recording of ancient knowledge. In practical sciences like chemistry and medicine, induction was combined with experiment. The author concludes that induction was a major tool for Muslim scholars, and notes its expanded role in modern Western science, emphasizing its practical utility for understanding the natural world and God’s laws.

Key Terms
الاستقراء
النحو
الصرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Deduction from universal principles
B) Induction from surveying actual speech
C) Intuition and personal judgment
D) Revelation from divine sources

Key Takeaway
Induction is the empirical foundation of Arabic grammatical sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Given three Arabic sentences: 'جاء زيد', 'ذهب عمرو', 'قامت هند', identify the pattern that suggests the rule for subject case (رفع).

Induction in Fiqh
Author lists fiqh areas relying on induction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Muslim jurists used induction to derive general juristic rules (qawa'id fiqhiyya) and specific rulings for water types, menstrual blood, and fetal duration.

Core Idea
Induction transforms scattered textual evidence into coherent fiqh rules.

This lesson examines the role of induction in Islamic jurisprudence. The passage shows that jurists inductively collected data on water purity, blood types, and fetal stay times before deriving legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify inductive applications in fiqh
The passage lists several fiqh areas: general rules, water rulings, blood rulings, fetal duration, and collection of actions.
Each involves gathering many particular cases or textual evidence before ruling.
2
Understand the inductive process for water
Jurists enumerated types of water (sea, well, river, etc.) and their properties, then derived rulings like 'water is pure unless changed'.
This is an inductive generalization from observed water samples.
3
Extend to other topics
Similarly, for menstrual blood, they collected reports on duration and color, then set legal limits.
Fetal duration was determined by surveying pregnancy cases known to the community.

Induction in Textual Research
Author describes induction in textual collection across disciplines

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the role of induction as the first step in textual research in hadith, history, and other Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Induction gathers the texts before interpretation begins.

This lesson focuses on how scholars used induction to collect relevant texts in hadith, history, geography, and literature before analyzing them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the inductive stage in research
The passage states that a researcher first collects texts related to his topic inductively.
'يعتمد أولاً على الاستقراء لجمع النصوص' – induction is the starting point.
2
Understand the scope of textual induction
It includes hadith, history, geography, wisdom, proverbs, ethics, and recording ancient knowledge.
This shows induction is not limited to one field.
3
Connect to ijtihad
After collection, the scholar exerts ijtihad to understand the texts using established principles.
Induction provides the raw material for interpretive reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلمون وطريق الاستقراء : وكان الاستقراء هو الوسيلة التي اعتمد عليها علماء المسلمين حين استخرجوا قواعد اللغة العربية وضوابطها، وقد كلفهم ذلك جهوداً مضنية، إذ استقصوا فيها معظم التراكيب العربية وطرق أدائها، ومعظم المفردات العربية ومشتقاتها، ثم استخرجوا من ذلك ضوابط النحو والصرف، فكانت هذه من العلوم التي توصل المسلمون إلى جلّ ما فيها عن طريق الاستقراء . وكان للاستقراء دور مهم في استخراج علم العروض الذي وضعه الخليل، وفي ضبط زحافاته وعلله، إذ تتبع الشعر العربي، وأحصى وضبط ما شاهد فيه . وكان الاستقراء أحد الوسائل التي اعتمد عليها فقهاء المسلمين في طائفة من أبواب الفقه الإسلامي . فبالاستقراء استخرجوا القواعد الفقهية العامة، وبالاستقراء أحصوا المياه ثم ضبطوا أحكامها الشرعية، وعليه اعتمدوا في تحديد دماء الحيض والنفاس والاستحاضة، وفي تحديد مقدار مكث الأجنة في الأرحام، ثم استنبطوا أحكامها الشرعية. وعلى الاستقراء اعتمدوا في جمع أعمال الناس الشخصية وغير الشخصية، ثم اجتهدوا في استخراج الحكم الشرعي لكل عمل منها. وحين يبحث باحثهم في النصوص فإنه يعتمد أولاً على الاستقراء لجمع النصوص التي تتعلق ببحثه، ثم ينظر فيما جمعه منها، ويجتهد في فهمها وفق طرائق الاجتهاد التي تُحدّدها له أصول الفهم والاستنباط. أما الاستقراء لمجرد جمع المعارف فقد كان وسيلتهم في الحديث الشريف، وفي التاريخ والجغرافيا، وفي الأدب والحِكَم والأمثال والأخلاق والآداب وتدوين معارف الأولين، وغير ذلك. وكان الاستقراء المقرون بالتجارب العملية وسيلتهم في الكيمياء وفي الطب، ضمن حدود الأدوات التي كانت متيسرة لهم في أزمانهم. من كل هذا نلاحظ أن الاستقراء وسيلة كبرى من الوسائل التي اعتمد عليها علماء المسلمين، فيما توصلوا إليه من معارف، وفيما دونوه من علوم. ومع تفجر الثورة العلمية المادية الحديثة، أخذ الاستقراء دوراً أكبر في العالم الغربي، وتحدّدت له مفاهيم أكثر دقة وأكثر ضبطاً من الناحية العملية، وكان له في مجال العلوم المادية واستخراج قوانينها المقام الأول، وطبيعي أن يكون له هذا المقام العظيم، لأنه هو الوسيلة العملية النافعة التي يستطيع الإنسان بها التوصل إلى معرفة خصائص الكون، وصفات الأشياء، وسنن الله الثابتة في العالم الطبيعي.
Muslims and the method of induction: Induction was the means that Muslim scholars relied on when they extracted the rules and principles of the Arabic language. It cost them strenuous efforts, as they surveyed most Arabic constructions and methods of expression, and most Arabic vocabulary and derivatives, then extracted from that the rules of grammar and morphology. These were among the sciences which Muslims reached most of their content through induction. Induction had an important role in developing the science of prosody (al-‘arud) established by al-Khalil, and in regulating its deviations (zihafat) and defects (‘ilal), as he tracked Arabic poetry, enumerated and recorded what he observed in it. Induction was one of the methods upon which Muslim jurists relied in a number of areas of Islamic jurisprudence. Through induction they extracted general juristic rules; through induction they enumerated types of water and then regulated their legal rulings; upon it they relied in determining the blood of menstruation, postpartum bleeding, and istihada, and in determining the duration of the stay of fetuses in the wombs, and then deduced their legal rulings. Upon induction they relied in collecting people's personal and impersonal actions, then exercised ijtihad to extract the legal ruling for each action. When a researcher examines texts, he first relies on induction to collect the texts relevant to his research, then examines what he has collected, and exerts ijtihad in understanding them according to the methods of ijtihad defined for him by the principles of understanding and derivation. As for induction for the mere collection of knowledge, it was their method in hadith, in history and geography, in literature, wisdom, proverbs, ethics, good manners, recording the knowledge of the ancients, and other fields. Induction combined with practical experiments was their method in chemistry and medicine, within the limits of the tools available to them in their times. From all this we observe that induction is a major tool among the means upon which Muslim scholars relied in the knowledge they attained and the sciences they recorded. With the eruption of the modern material scientific revolution, induction took a greater role in the Western world, and its concepts became more precise and more rigorous practically, and it occupied the foremost position in the field of material sciences and the extraction of their laws. Naturally it has this great position, because it is the practical useful method by which man can attain knowledge of the properties of the universe, the qualities of things, and the steadfast laws of Allah in the natural world.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a historical-epistemological exposition of the role of induction (istiqra’) in Islamic civilization. The author begins by stating that Muslim scholars relied on induction to extract the rules of Arabic grammar and morphology, surveying a vast corpus of speech. He then mentions al-Khalil’s derivation of ‘arud (prosody) by inductively examining Arabic poetry. Next, he turns to fiqh: jurists used induction to formulate general legal principles (qawa‘id fiqhiyya), classify types of water and their rulings, determine menstrual and fetal periods, and collect actions for ijtihad. In textual research, induction is the first step for gathering relevant texts before interpretation. The author extends the method to hadith, history, geography, literature, ethics, and the recording of ancient knowledge. In practical sciences like chemistry and medicine, induction was combined with experiment. The author concludes that induction was a major tool for Muslim scholars, and notes its expanded role in modern Western science, emphasizing its practical utility for understanding the natural world and God’s laws.

النصوص
الاجتهاد

According to the passage, what is the first step a researcher takes when examining texts?
A) Immediate interpretation

C) Analogical reasoning
D) Consultation with scholars

Key Takeaway
Induction is the foundational method for gathering evidence in textual Islamic sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are researching the ruling on riba. Use induction to list all verses and hadith that mention riba or usury before deriving a ruling.
?

Induction in Empirical Sciences
Author discusses induction in empirical sciences and its modern significance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the use of induction by Muslim scholars in chemistry and medicine with its modern role in Western science.

Core Idea
Induction combined with experiment unlocks the laws of nature.

This lesson covers the empirical application of induction in chemistry and medicine by Muslim scholars, and notes its expansion in the modern scientific revolution.

Guided Path
1
Recognize induction in early Islamic chemistry
Muslim chemists used induction together with practical experiments within their available tools.
The passage says 'الاستقراء المقرون بالتجارب العملية'.
2
Understand induction in medicine
Physicians inductively collected observations about diseases, treatments, and natural properties.
This parallels modern clinical research.
3
Compare with modern science
The author notes that with the modern scientific revolution, induction became more precise and central in the West.
Induction is described as the practical method to know the properties of the universe.

Induction and Analogy

Induction in Islamic Scholarship and Its Quranic Basis
Author describes the use of induction by Muslim scholars and its Quranic foundations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the role of induction in classical Islamic scholarship and identify its Quranic foundations as presented by the author.

Core Idea
Induction is not a modern Western import but a foundational Islamic method, rooted in the Quran.

This passage from the author's work illuminates how Muslim scholars across disciplines—from fiqh to history to medicine—relied on induction as a primary means of acquiring knowledge. Moreover, the author demonstrates that the Quran itself commands the use of induction, both complete and incomplete, for deriving laws of nature and divine patterns.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disciplines
Read the passage and list the fields where Muslim scholars applied induction according to the author. Look for mentions of fiqh (legal rulings), hadith, history, chemistry, medicine, etc.
2
Distinguish complete vs. incomplete induction
The author mentions both types. Note that complete induction (istiqra' tamm) is exhaustive but rarely attainable, while incomplete induction (istiqra' naqis) from limited events is deemed sufficient for deriving sunan (God's ways).
This distinction is key to understanding Islamic epistemology of induction.
3
Examine the Quranic justification
Identify the Quranic verses the author cites (e.g., Surat al-‘Ankabut 29:20). Understand how the command to 'travel in the earth' and observe particulars is interpreted as an inductive method for deriving universal truths about creation and God's consistent pattern.
4
Analyze the logic of incomplete induction
The author argues that incomplete induction works because God's wisdom is consistent. From limited historical events (e.g., destruction of tyrants), one can infer a general law that similar actions will lead to similar consequences. This is based on tawhid and divine justice.
This reasoning is foundational for qiyas (analogical reasoning) in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ضبطوا أحكامها الشرعية، وعليه اعتمدوا في تحديد دماء الحيض والنفاس والاستحاضة، وفي تحديد مقدار مكث الأجنة في الأرحام، ثم استنبطوا أحكامها الشرعية.
وعلى الاستقراء اعتمدوا في جمع أعمال الناس الشخصية وغير الشخصية، ثم اجتهدوا في استخراج الحكم الشرعي لكل عمل منها.
وحين يبحث باحثهم في النصوص فإنه يعتمد أولاً على الاستقراء لجمع النصوص التي تتعلق ببحثه، ثم ينظر فيما جمعه منها، ويجتهد في فهمها وفق طرائق الاجتهاد التي تُحدّدها له أصول الفهم والاستنباط.
أما الاستقراء لمجرد جمع المعارف فقد كان وسيلتهم في الحديث الشريف، وفي التاريخ والجغرافيا، وفي الأدب والحِكَم والأمثال والأخلاق والآداب وتدوين معارف الأولين، وغير ذلك.
وكان الاستقراء المقرون بالتجارب العملية وسيلتهم في الكيمياء وفي الطب، ضمن حدود الأدوات التي كانت متيسرة لهم في أزمانهم.
من كل هذا نلاحظ أن الاستقراء وسيلة كبرى من الوسائل التي اعتمد عليها علماء المسلمين، فيما توصلوا إليه من معارف، وفيما دونوه من علوم.
ومع تفجر الثورة العلمية المادية الحديثة، أخذ الاستقراء دوراً أكبر في العالم الغربي، وتحدّدت له مفاهيم أكثر دقة وأكثر ضبطاً من الناحية العملية، وكان له في مجال العلوم المادية واستخراج قوانينها المقام الأول، وطبيعي أن يكون له هذا المقام العظيم، لأنه هو الوسيلة العملية النافعة التي يستطيع الإنسان بها التوصل إلى معرفة خصائص الكون، وصفات الأشياء، وسنن الله الثابتة في العالم الطبيعي.
القرآن والاستقراء:
لا يخفى على الباحث في كتاب الله ما يشتمل عليه هذا الكتاب من دفع إلى اتخاذ طريق الاستقراء - بقسميه التام والناقص - وسيلة إلى تحصيل كثير من المعارف.
فحين يوجه القرآن إلى الاتعاظ والاعتبار بقصص الأولين، فإنه يلفت النظر إلى طائفة من الأحداث التي جرت للأمم السابقة، واعتبار هذه الأحداث كافية للتنبيه على سنة الله في عباده، والاتعاظ بها والاعتبار.
ولا يكون هذا إلا على أساس استفادة قواعد كلية وسنن عامة، من أحداث محدودة لم تبلغ مبلغ الاستقراء التام، لكنها تجعل الفكر يقيس ما سيأتي على ما مضى، نظراً إلى أن مدبّر الكون واحد، وأن حكمته التي قضت فيما مضى بإهلاك من طغى وبغى، تقضي فيما يأتي بإهلاك من يفعل فعل السابقين، وأن حكمته التي قضت فيما مضى بنصرة المؤمنين المتقين، تقضي فيما يأتي بنصرة المؤمنين المتقين، إذا قاموا بما أوجب الله عليهم من إعداد وعمل وجهاد.
والنصوص القرآنية في هذا المجال كثيرة.
وحين يوجه القرآن إلى دراسة الطبيعة لمعرفة كيف بدأ الله الخلق، فإنه يوجه إلى طريقة الاستقراء، بالسير في الأرض وتتبع دراسة الجزئيات الكونية، لتكون هذه الجزئيات هادية لهم إلى معرفة الحقيقة الكلية.
وفي هذا يقول الله تعالى في سورة (العنكبوت ٢٩): أَوَلَمْ يَرَوْا كَيْفَ يُبْدِئُ اللَّهُ الْخَلْقَ ثُمَّ يُعِيدُهُ إِنَّ ذَلِكَ عَلَى اللَّهِ يَسِيرٌ قُلْ سِيرُوا فِي الْأَرْضِ فَانظُرُوا كَيْفَ بَدَأَ الْخَلْقَ ثُمَّ اللَّهُ يُنشِئُ النَّشْأَةَ الْآخِرَةَ إِنَّ اللَّهَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرٌ .
ويقتضي هذا السير في الأرض الذي أمر الله به تتبع الجزئيات، ودراسة تكوينها، ودراسة نشأتها، لاستنتاج القوانين والقواعد الكلية التي تبين لهم كيف بدأ الله الخلق، وهذا هو منهج الاستقراء بعينه.
Then they established their legal rulings, and relied on it in determining the blood of menstruation, postpartum bleeding, and istihadah (non-menstrual uterine bleeding), and in determining the duration of fetuses in wombs, and then they derived their legal rulings. And upon induction they relied in collecting the personal and non-personal actions of people, then they exerted effort in extracting the legal ruling for each action. And when a researcher among them investigates texts, he first relies on induction to gather the texts related to his research, then he examines what he has collected from them, and strives to understand them according to the methods of ijtihad determined for him by the principles of understanding and deduction. As for induction merely for collecting knowledge, it was their means in hadith, history, geography, literature, proverbs, ethics, manners, recording the knowledge of predecessors, and other things. And induction coupled with practical experiments was their means in chemistry and medicine, within the limits of the tools available to them in their times. From all this we note that induction is a major means upon which Muslim scholars relied in what they attained of knowledge and in what they recorded of sciences. And with the eruption of the modern material scientific revolution, induction took a greater role in the Western world, and its concepts became more precise and more controlled from a practical standpoint, and it held the foremost position in the field of material sciences and extracting their laws, and it is natural that it holds this great position, because it is the beneficial practical means by which man can attain knowledge of the properties of the universe, the attributes of things, and the established laws of God in the natural world. The Quran and Induction: It is not hidden from the researcher in the Book of God what this Book contains of urging to adopt the path of induction – with its two types, complete and incomplete – as a means to acquire much knowledge. For when the Quran directs to heed and take lesson from the stories of the predecessors, it draws attention to a group of events that occurred for previous nations, and considering these events sufficient to alert to God's sunnah (way) among His servants, to heed them and take lesson. And this is only on the basis of deriving universal rules and general laws from limited events that did not reach the level of complete induction, but they make the mind measure what is to come by what has passed, given that the Governor of the universe is One, and that His wisdom which decreed in the past the destruction of those who transgressed and oppressed, decrees in the future the destruction of those who do the deeds of the predecessors, and that His wisdom which decreed in the past the victory of the believing righteous, decrees in the future the victory of the believing righteous, if they fulfill what God obligated upon them of preparation, action, and jihad. And the Quranic texts in this field are many. And when the Quran directs to the study of nature to know how God began creation, it directs to the method of induction, by traveling in the earth and pursuing the study of cosmic particulars, so that these particulars may guide them to knowledge of the universal truth. And in this God Almighty says in Surat al-‘Ankabut (29): 'Have they not seen how God begins creation, then repeats it? Indeed, that is easy for God. Say: Travel through the earth and see how He began creation. Then God will produce the final creation. Indeed, God is over all things competent.' And this travel in the earth commanded by God requires following the particulars, studying their composition and their origin, to deduce the universal laws and rules that show them how God began creation, and this is the method of induction itself.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion on the role of induction (استقراء) in Islamic scholarship and its Quranic justification. The author begins by describing how Muslim scholars relied on induction in deriving legal rulings (fiqh), specifically in matters like menstrual blood, postpartum bleeding, and fetal gestation periods. They also used induction to collect and categorize human actions to extract legal rulings. In textual research, a scholar first collects relevant texts via induction, then studies and interprets them according to established principles of ijtihad. Beyond law, induction was used in hadith, history, geography, literature, ethics, and recording ancient knowledge. In empirical sciences like chemistry and medicine, induction was coupled with practical experiments within their technological limits. The author concludes that induction was a major method for Muslim scholars. Then, discussing the modern era, he notes its expanded role in the West for material sciences, describing induction as the beneficial means to understand the universe's properties and God's natural laws. Next, the author turns to the Quran's encouragement of induction. He states that the Quran urges two types of induction: complete (تام) and incomplete (ناقص). When the Quran directs to heed the stories of past nations, it draws attention to limited events that are considered sufficient to understand God's sunnah. This involves deriving universal laws from incomplete induction, reasoning from past to future because God's wisdom is consistent. The author cites the Quranic command to travel and observe how God began creation (Surat al-‘Ankabut 29:20), which requires studying particulars to deduce universal principles about creation. This, he says, is exactly the method of induction. The passage highlights that induction is not only a modern scientific method but was central to Islamic scholarship and divinely ordained.

Induction and Analogy
intermediate

The Quranic Method of Induction (Istiqra')
Author explains how the Quran directs believers to use induction to derive general laws from history and nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of induction (complete and incomplete) as used in Islamic epistemology and explain how the Quran directs believers to use incomplete induction to derive universal laws from historical and natural particulars.

Core Idea
Induction in the Quran derives divine sunan from limited evidence, relying on God's consistent wisdom.

In classical Islamic logic, induction (istiqra') is often treated as a weaker form of argument compared to syllogism. However, this passage from Dawabit al-Ma'rifa demonstrates that the Quran itself commands the use of induction as a valid method for acquiring knowledge of universal laws. By studying history and nature, believers are led to understand Allah's consistent patterns.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic basis for induction
Find the two Quranic directives mentioned: (1) heeding the stories of past nations to derive universal lessons, and (2) traveling through the earth to observe nature and deduce how Allah began creation.
The passage cites Surah al-Ankabut 29 as a key verse for the observational method.
2
Distinguish complete vs. incomplete induction
Complete induction (istiqra' tam) examines every instance, while incomplete induction (istiqra' naqis) uses a sample. The passage emphasizes that exhaustive enumeration is not required; studying diverse samples suffices.
Incomplete induction yields probability (zann), not certainty, but is still epistemically valuable.
3
Understand the justification for incomplete induction
The author argues that because God's wisdom is one and the universe is governed by strict general laws, studying a sample can lead to preponderant probability about universal patterns. This is the core epistemological justification.
The possibility of exceptions remains, but probability favors uniformity.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider how jurists derive the general rule that all intoxicants are forbidden from specific prohibitions like khamr. This uses incomplete induction based on the common 'illa of intoxication.
This shows how induction operates in Usul al-Fiqh.

دفع إلى اتخاذ طريق الاستقراء - بقسميه التام والناقص - وسيلة إلى تحصيل كثير من المعارف. فحين يوجه القرآن إلى الاتعاظ والاعتبار بقصص الأولين، فإنه يلفت النظر إلى طائفة من الأحداث التي جرت للأمم السابقة، واعتبار هذه الأحداث كافية للتنبيه على سنة الله في عباده، والاتعاظ بها والاعتبار. ولا يكون هذا إلا على أساس استفادة قواعد كلية وسنن عامة، من أحداث محدودة لم تبلغ مبلغ الاستقراء التام، لكنها تجعل الفكر يقيس ما سيأتي على ما مضى، نظراً إلى أن مدبر الكون واحد، وأن حكمته التي قضت فيما مضى بإهلاك من طغى وبغى، تقضي فيما يأتي بإهلاك من يفعل فعل السابقين، وأن حكمته التي قضت فيما مضى بنصرة المؤمنين المتقين، تقضي فيما يأتي بنصرة المؤمنين المتقين، إذا قاموا بما أوجب الله عليهم من إعداد وعمل وجهاد. والنصوص القرآنية في هذا المجال كثيرة. وحين يوجه القرآن إلى دراسة الطبيعة لمعرفة كيف بدأ الله الخلق، فإنه يوجه إلى طريقة الاستقراء، بالسير في الأرض وتتبع دراسة الجزئيات الكونية، لتكون هذه الجزئيات هادية لهم إلى معرفة الحقيقة الكلية. وفي هذا يقول الله تعالى في سورة (العنكبوت ٢٩): أَوَلَمْ يَرَوْا كَيْفَ يُبْدِئُ اللَّهُ الْخَلْقَ ثُمَّ يُعِيدُهُ إِنَّ ذَلِكَ عَلَى اللَّهِ يَسِيرٌ قُلْ سِيرُوا فِي الْأَرْضِ فَانظُرُوا كَيْفَ بَدَأَ الْخَلْقَ ثُمَّ اللَّهُ يُنشِئُ النَّشْأَةَ الْآخِرَةَ إِنَّ اللَّهَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرٌ . ويقتضي هذا السير في الأرض الذي أمر الله به تتبع الجزئيات، ودراسة تكوينها، ودراسة نشأتها، لاستنتاج القوانين والقواعد الكلية التي تبين لهم كيف بدأ الله الخلق، وهذا هو منهج الاستقراء بعينه. ولا يلزم من التتبع الاستقصاء، بل قد يكفي الباحث أن يدرس نماذج متنوعة، يستنبط منها كليات عامة، ويقيس ما لم يدرسه على ما درسه، نظراً إلى أن الملاحظ في الكون بوجه عام، أنه تهيمن عليه قوانين عامة صارمة، فدراسة بعض الجزئيات قد يدل الفكر على قانونها العام الشامل لها ولأشباهها، ولئن ظل احتمال مخالفة ما لم يُدرس لما درس احتمالاً قائماً، إلا أن غلبة الظن ترجح انتظام كل الجزئيات تحت قانون واحد، قد يصل الباحث إليه كله، أو إلى بعضه. ومتابعة البحث كفيلة بوصول الإنسان في يوم ما إلى الحقيقة النهائية، في طائفة من الموضوعات التي أعطاه الله مفاتيح بحثها.
It incites to adopt the method of induction - in its two types, complete and incomplete - as a means to acquire many branches of knowledge. When the Quran directs to heed and take warning from the stories of previous peoples, it draws attention to a group of events that occurred to past nations, and the consideration of these events is sufficient to alert to Allah's sunna among His servants, and to heed and take warning thereby. This can only be based on deriving universal rules and general sunan from limited events that do not reach the level of complete induction, but they make the mind measure what is to come by what has passed, considering that the Director of the universe is One, and that His wisdom which decreed in the past the destruction of those who transgressed and rebelled decrees in the future the destruction of whoever does the deeds of the predecessors, and that His wisdom which decreed in the past the victory of the believing righteous decrees in the future the victory of the believing righteous, if they carry out what Allah obligated upon them of preparation, work, and jihad. And the Quranic texts in this regard are many. And when the Quran directs to study nature to know how Allah began creation, it directs to the method of induction, by traveling in the earth and following the study of cosmic particulars, so that these particulars may guide them to knowledge of the universal truth. And in this Allah the Exalted says in Surah al-Ankabut 29: 'And have they not seen how Allah begins creation and then repeats it? Indeed that is easy for Allah. Say: travel through the earth and see how He began creation. Then Allah will bring forth the final creation. Indeed Allah over all things is All-Powerful.' And this travel in the earth which Allah commanded requires following the particulars, studying their formation, and studying their genesis, to deduce the universal laws and rules that show them how Allah began creation, and this is precisely the method of induction. And exhaustive pursuit is not necessary; rather it may suffice for the researcher to study diverse samples, from which he deduces general universals, and measures what he has not studied by what he has studied, considering that it is observed in the universe generally that strict general laws dominate it. So the study of some particulars may lead the mind to their general law inclusive of them and their likes. And even if the possibility of contradiction of what has not been studied to what has been studied remains a standing possibility, yet the preponderance of opinion favors the regulation of all particulars under one law, the whole or part of which the researcher may arrive at. And the pursuit of research is sufficient for man to reach, one day, the final truth in a group of topics whose keys Allah has given him to research.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the Islamic epistemological method of induction (istiqra') as derived from Quranic guidance. The author begins by stating that induction in its two forms, complete and incomplete, is a means of acquiring knowledge. He then explains how the Quran uses historical narratives to teach that from limited events one can derive universal divine laws (sunan), because God's wisdom is consistent. This is an example of incomplete induction. Next, the author cites the Quranic command to travel and observe nature (Surah al-Ankabut) as a directive to study particulars in order to infer universal truths about creation. He emphasizes that exhaustive enumeration is not required; studying diverse samples suffices. This is justified by the observation that the universe is governed by strict general laws. While acknowledging a possibility of exceptions, the author holds that preponderant probability (ghalabat al-zann) supports uniformity. Continuous research will ultimately lead to final truth in fields where God has provided the means. This passage shows that induction is not a foreign concept but a method rooted in Islamic scripture and reasoning. For example, from observing that intoxicants cause intoxication and that the Prophet forbade khamr, one can inductively conclude that all intoxicants are forbidden, even if not every intoxicant has been individually tested.

Complete and Incomplete Induction
Author defines and exemplifies complete and incomplete induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between complete and incomplete induction and determine which yields certainty.

Core Idea
Certainty requires examining every case; otherwise, only probability.

Induction is a fundamental method in Islamic sciences for deriving general rules from particulars. In this passage, the author introduces two types: complete induction, which examines all instances, and incomplete induction, which examines only some. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the epistemic strength of arguments in fiqh, kalam, and other disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Find the Arabic terms for 'complete induction' and 'incomplete induction' in the passage. Note the definitions given.
Look for 'الاستقراء التام' and 'الاستقراء الناقص'.
2
Extract the defining criteria
For each type, note what makes it complete or incomplete: complete induction encompasses all particulars; incomplete induction studies only some.
3
Analyze the examples
List the examples given for complete induction (e.g., units of a day, solar year, Pleiades stars). Verify that each example indeed covers all possible instances.
These are cases where exhaustive enumeration is possible.
4
Assess epistemic yield
Contrast the certainty (yaqin) of complete induction with the probability (zann) of incomplete induction. Consider why incomplete induction cannot give certainty.
The passage states that complete induction may yield certainty if it reaches that level.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يلزم من التتبع الاستقصاء، بل قد يكفي الباحث أن يدرس نماذج متنوعة، يستنبط منها كليات عامة، ويقيس ما لم يدرسه على ما درسه، نظراً إلى أن الملاحظ في الكون بوجه عام، أنه تهيمن عليه قوانين عامة صارمة، فدراسة بعض الجزئيات قد يدل الفكر على قانونها العام الشامل لها ولأشباهها، ولئن ظل احتمال مخالفة ما لم يُدرس لما درس احتمالاً قائماً، إلا أن غلبة الظن تُرَجّح انتظام كل الجزئيات تحت قانون واحد، قد يصل الباحث إليه كله، أو إلى بعضه. ومتابعة البحث كفيلة بوصول الإنسان في يوم ما إلى الحقيقة النهائية، في طائفة من الموضوعات التي أعطاه الله مفاتيح بحثها.
أَقْسَامُ الاسْتِقْرَاء
ظهر لنا مما سبق أن الاستقراء تامّ وناقص، فهو إذن قسمان:
القسم الأول: الاستقراء التام، وهو الذي يتم فيه استيعاب جميع جزئيات أو أجزاء الشيء الذي هو موضوع البحث، بالنظر والدراسة العلمية، وفق المستوى الذي يتطلبه البحث العلمي.
فبالاستقراء التام عرفنا وحدات الزمن التي ينقسم إليها اليوم الكامل بليله ونهاره، وعرفنا وحدات الزمن التي ينقسم إليها العام الشمسي، ووحدات الزمن التي ينقسم إليها العام القمري، ما دام نظام الكون مستمراً على ما هو عليه الآن وما كان عليه قبل الآن.
وبالاستقراء التام عرف الفلكيون عدد نجوم الثريا، وعرفوا الآن المجموعة الشمسية، وعرف الجغرافيون الأنهر الكبرى في الأرض، وعرفوا القارات، وعرف الكيميائيون العناصر الأساسية التي يتكون منها الماء.
وهكذا نلاحظ أن كثيراً من المعارف العلمية قد توصل إليها الإنسان ويتوصل إليها عن طريق الاستقراء التام.
والاستقراء التام قد يفيد اليقين، وذلك إذا بلغت المعرفة بسببه مبلغ اليقين.
القسم الثاني: الاستقراء الناقص، وهو الذي تدرس فيه بعض جزئيات أو أجزاء الشيء الذي هو موضوع البحث. وتعتبر فيه النماذج المدروسة أساساً
And exhaustive tracing is not necessary; rather, the researcher may suffice to study diverse samples, deduce from them general universals, and measure what he has not studied against what he has studied, considering that observation in the universe in general is that it is dominated by strict general laws. So studying some particulars may indicate to the mind their general law that encompasses them and their likes. Even if the possibility of what is not studied differing from what is studied remains a standing possibility, yet preponderance of opinion favors the regulation of all particulars under a single law, which the researcher may reach entirely or partially. And following up research is sufficient for man to reach the final truth one day, in a group of topics for which God gave him the keys to investigate.
Divisions of Induction
It has become clear to us from the foregoing that induction is complete and incomplete; thus it is two types:
The first type: Complete induction, which is the one in which all particulars or parts of the thing that is the subject of research are encompassed, through observation and scientific study, according to the level required by scientific research.
By complete induction we knew the units of time into which the full day with its night and day is divided, and we knew the units of time into which the solar year is divided, and the units of time into which the lunar year is divided, as long as the system of the universe continues as it is now and as it was before now.
And by complete induction astronomers knew the number of stars in the Pleiades, and they now know the solar system, and geographers knew the major rivers on earth, and they knew the continents, and chemists knew the basic elements that constitute water.
Thus we observe that much scientific knowledge has been reached by man and is reached through complete induction.
And complete induction may yield certainty, if knowledge by means of it reaches the level of certainty.
The second type: Incomplete induction, which is the one in which some particulars or parts of the thing that is the subject of research are studied. And the studied samples are considered the basis.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies induction (istiqra') into two types: complete induction (al-istiqra' al-tamm) and incomplete induction (al-istiqra' al-naqis). The author begins by explaining that exhaustive examination is not always necessary; a researcher can study diverse samples, derive general laws, and apply them to unstudied cases, relying on the preponderance of opinion (ghalabat al-zann) that everything falls under a single law. Then the author explicitly states the division. Complete induction encompasses all particulars or parts of the subject. Examples include knowing the units of a full day, solar and lunar years, the number of stars in the Pleiades, the solar system, major rivers, continents, and the elements of water. These are cases where exhaustive enumeration is possible. Complete induction can yield certainty (yaqin). Incomplete induction studies only some particulars and uses them as a basis for generalization. This is the kind that yields probability, not certainty. The author follows a clear enumerative structure: first type, then second. All examples are scientific, but the same logic applies to Islamic sciences (e.g., surveying all companions for a consensus or sampling some for predominant opinion).

الاستقراء الناقص

Induction and Analogy
beginner

Definition and Division of Induction into Complete and Incomplete
Author defines and classifies induction into complete and incomplete with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between complete and incomplete induction and identify which type is used in examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Induction becomes certain when all cases are checked; otherwise it yields probability, not certainty.

Induction is a fundamental method of reasoning in Islamic sciences, from identifying the 'illah in qiyas to deriving general principles from revealed texts. In this passage, the author defines induction and divides it into two types: complete (tamm) and incomplete (naqis). Understanding this division is crucial because it determines the epistemic value—certainty or probability—of your conclusions. Let's work through the criteria that distinguish these two types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus and species
The author states that induction is of two kinds: complete and incomplete. The genus is 'induction' (istiqra'), a method of reasoning from particulars. The two species are defined by whether all particulars are surveyed or only some.
2
Extract the condition for complete induction
Complete induction requires surveying 'all particulars or parts of the thing' that is the subject of research. When this is done, the conclusion can reach certainty (yaqin). Note that the author gives examples from astronomy, geography, and chemistry.
In Islamic sciences, complete induction might apply when all verses of a ruling are known or all hadiths on a topic are collected.
3
Extract the condition for incomplete induction
Incomplete induction studies only some particulars and generalizes based on the studied samples. The conclusion is probable (zanni), relying on the assumption of uniformity or similarity between studied and unstudied cases.
The author emphasizes that incomplete induction is the basis for most practical knowledge and natural laws.
4
Apply the distinction to a Fiqh example
Consider the ruling on intoxicants. The prohibition of khamr (wine) is known from revelation. To extend this to other intoxicants, jurists use incomplete induction: they observe that many intoxicants cause intoxication, so they induce that intoxication is the 'illah (effective cause). This is incomplete because not all intoxicants have been tested, yet the ruling is applied to all based on similarity.
This example shows the practical importance of knowing which type of induction is used.

ظهر لنا مما سبق أن الاستقراء تامّ وناقص، فهو إذن قسمان:

فبالاستقراء التام عرفنا وحدات الزمن التي ينقسم إليها اليوم الكامل بليله ونهاره، وعرفنا وحدات الزمن التي ينقسم إليها العام الشمسي، ووحدات الزمن التي ينقسم إليها العام القمري، ما دام نظام الكون مستمراً على ما هو عليه الآن وما كان عليه قبل الآن.
وبالاستقراء التام عرف الفلكيون عدد نجوم الثريا، وعرفوا الآن المجموعة الشمسية، وعرف الجغرافيون الأنهر الكبرى في الأرض، وعرفوا القارات، وعرف الكيميائيون العناصر الأساسية التي يتكون منها الماء.
وهكذا نلاحظ أن كثيراً من المعارف العلمية قد توصل إليها الإنسان ويتوصل إليها عن طريق الاستقراء التام.
والاستقراء التام قد يفيد اليقين، وذلك إذا بلغت المعرفة بسببه مبلغ اليقين.
القسم الثاني: الاستقراء الناقص، وهو الذي تدرس فيه بعض جزئيات أو أجزاء الشيء الذي هو موضوع البحث. وتعتبر فيه النماذج المدروسة أساساً تقاس عليه بقية الأجزاء أو الجزئيات، وبالاستناد إلى ذلك يُصْدِر الباحث حكماً عاماً ظنياً يشمل ما درسه، وما لم يدرسه، بناء على غلبة ظنه بأن نظام الكون ذو قوانين كلية يندر فيها الشذوذ ما دام النوع المدروس واحداً، أو بناء على غلبة ظنه بأن بقية الأجزاء مشابهة للأجزاء التي درسها.
فحين يُراد معرفة ما في دم الإنسان من عناصر أصيلة ودخيلة، تدرس منه كمية قليلة، ثم يعطي الطبيب المحلّل حكمه على الدم كله بالاستناد إلى الكمية التي درسها.
وحين يراد معرفة نوع تربة الأرض الزراعية وعناصرها وخصائصها، تؤخذ من أماكن متعددة منها نماذج، ويجري عليها التحليل والفحص المخبري، ثم يُصْدِر المهندس المحلل حكمه على كل تربة هذه الأرض، بالاستناد إلى النماذج التي درسها.
ومعظم القوانين الطبيعية التي توصل إليها الباحثون الطبيعيون، إنما توصلوا إليها عن طريق الاستقراء الناقص.
والناس من قديم الزمان لديهم أحكام كلية عامة، استندوا فيها إلى النتائج التي تَوَصَّلوا إليها بالاستقراء الناقص، وكانت أحكامهم هذه مفيدة ونافعة.
فالتجارب أوضحت لهم أن السمَّ قاتل، فأصدروا حكماً عاماً على السم بأنه قاتل، مع أن استقراءهم قد كان ناقصاً غير تام، ولكنهم قاسوا ما لم يتصفحوه على ما تصفحوه، بحكم التشابه بين الجزء المدروس والجزء غير المدروس.
والتجارب أوضحت لهم أن بعض العقاقير مسهلة، وبعضها قابضة، وبعضها نافعة للكبد، وبعضها مقوية للأعصاب، إلى غير ذلك، فأصدروا أحكاماً عامة كلية بالنسبة إليها، وكان مستندهم ما أجروه من استقراء ناقص حولها.
Sections of Induction
It became clear to us from the preceding that induction is complete and incomplete, so it is two divisions:
The first division: Complete induction, which is that in which all particulars or parts of the thing that is the subject of research are surveyed, by observation and scientific study, according to the level required by scientific research.
By complete induction we came to know the units of time into which the complete day with its night and day is divided, and we came to know the units of time into which the solar year is divided, and the units of time into which the lunar year is divided, as long as the system of the universe continues as it is now and as it was before now.
By complete induction astronomers knew the number of stars of the Pleiades, and now know the solar system, and geographers knew the major rivers on earth, and knew the continents, and chemists knew the basic elements that make up water.
Thus we observe that much scientific knowledge has been reached and is reached by man through complete induction.
Complete induction may yield certainty, when knowledge through it reaches the level of certainty.
The second division: Incomplete induction, which is that in which some particulars or parts of the thing that is the subject of research are studied. The studied samples are considered a basis upon which the remaining parts or particulars are measured, and based on that the researcher issues a probable general judgment that includes what he studied and what he did not study, based on his predominant opinion that the system of the universe has universal laws in which anomaly is rare as long as the studied type is one, or based on his predominant opinion that the remaining parts are similar to the parts he studied.
So when one wants to know what is in human blood of original and foreign elements, a small amount of it is studied, then the analyzing doctor gives his judgment on all the blood based on the amount he studied.
And when one wants to know the type of agricultural soil of a land and its elements and characteristics, samples are taken from multiple places from it, and laboratory analysis and examination are conducted on them, then the analyzing engineer issues his judgment on all the soil of that land, based on the samples he studied.
Most of the natural laws that natural researchers have reached were reached through incomplete induction.
People from ancient times have general universal judgments, in which they relied on the results they reached through incomplete induction, and these judgments were beneficial and useful.
Experiments showed them that poison is lethal, so they issued a general judgment on poison that it is lethal, even though their induction was incomplete not complete, but they measured what they had not examined on what they had examined, by virtue of the similarity between the studied part and the unstudied part.
And experiments showed them that some drugs are laxative, some are astringent, some are beneficial for the liver, and some are strengthening for the nerves, and so on, so they issued general universal judgments regarding them, and their basis was what they conducted of incomplete induction about them.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies induction (istiqra') into two types: complete (tamm) and incomplete (naqis). The author begins by stating that induction is of two kinds, then elaborates each.

First, complete induction: This involves surveying all particulars or parts of the subject matter through scientific observation. The author gives examples from various sciences: units of time (day, solar year, lunar year), the number of stars in the Pleiades, the solar system, major rivers, continents, and the composition of water. The key point is that complete induction can yield certainty (yaqin) if the survey is exhaustive. In Islamic sciences, complete induction might be applied to enumerating the verses of a surah or the conditions of a legal ruling when all instances are known.

Second, incomplete induction: This studies only some particulars and generalizes based on the studied samples. The researcher issues a probable (zanni) judgment, relying on the assumption that the universe follows consistent laws with rare anomalies, or that unstudied parts resemble studied ones. The author illustrates with examples: analyzing a blood sample to judge all blood, testing soil samples to judge an entire field, and most natural laws derived from incomplete induction. Historical examples include the judgment that poison is lethal and the medicinal properties of drugs—these were based on incomplete induction yet proved useful.

The passage emphasizes that incomplete induction provides probable knowledge (zann) and is the basis for most practical knowledge. In Islamic legal reasoning (usul al-fiqh), incomplete induction underlies analogical reasoning (qiyas), where a ruling from a studied case is extended to an unstudied case due to shared effective cause ('illa). For instance, the prohibition of wine is extended to other intoxicants based on the observed effect of intoxication.

Overall, the passage clarifies the epistemological status of each type: complete induction yields certainty when exhaustive, while incomplete induction yields probability and is pragmatically valuable.

Key Terms
استقراء
استقراء تام
استقراء ناقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Based on the passage, which type of induction can yield certainty (yaqin)?
A) Complete induction only
B) Incomplete induction only
C) Both complete and incomplete induction
D) Neither complete nor incomplete induction

Key Takeaway
Induction is divided into complete (certain when all cases are surveyed) and incomplete (probable when only some cases are studied).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether each of the following usul-al-fiqh reasonings uses complete or incomplete induction: (a) The jurist collects all known hadiths on the prohibition of riba and finds that every instance involves excess in exchange of two same items; he concludes riba is defined as any excess in such exchanges. (b) The jurist studies five cases of intoxicating beverages and observes that they all cause intoxication; he generalizes that all intoxicating beverages are forbidden because they share the 'illah of intoxication.
?
Provide your own example from Fiqh or Tafsir where incomplete induction is used to derive a general ruling, and explain why the induction is incomplete (i.e., not all particulars were surveyed).
