Induction and Analogy
beginner
8 min
Incomplete Induction: Part to Whole
Author defines incomplete induction and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify incomplete induction and construct an example from an Islamic science.

Core Idea
From a few cases to a probable general rule.

Incomplete induction is a core reasoning tool in empirical sciences. It works by examining a sample and then issuing a judgment about the entire class. This lesson unpacks its definition through concrete examples, showing how it operates in fields like medicine and agriculture.

Guided Path
1
Identify the method
Read the first two sentences of the Arabic passage. The author describes a process: studying some parts, then issuing a general judgment based on similarity. Highlight the key terms: 'يقاس عليه' (measured against it) and 'حكماً عاما ظنياً' (probable general judgment).
The method relies on the assumption that unexamined parts resemble examined ones.
2
Extract the examples
Find the examples of blood analysis and soil testing. Notice how the doctor and engineer each study a limited sample to judge the whole. This is the core move of incomplete induction.
These examples are from modern sciences, but the same logic applies in Islamic medicine.
3
Formalize the inductive argument
Represent the blood example as an inductive inference: Premise 1: Some blood samples (studied) contain elements X, Y, Z. Conclusion: All blood (including unstudied) contains those elements. This is incomplete induction because the conclusion goes beyond the premises.
The strength depends on how representative the sample is.
4
Apply to an Islamic science
Consider how a physician in Islamic civilization (like Ibn Sina) would use this method to determine the properties of a herb by testing it on a few patients and then generalizing to all cases. This mirrors the logic of the passage.
This method is foundational in Islamic medicine (tibb) and pharmacy.

ناقصاً غير تام، ولكنهم قاسوا ما لم يتصفحوه على ما تصفحوه، بحكم التشابه بين الجزء المدروس والجزء غير المدروس. والتجارب أوضحت لهم أن بعض العقاقير مسهلة، وبعضها قابضة، وبعضها نافعة للكبد، وبعضها مقوية للأعصاب، إلى غير ذلك، فأصدروا أحكاماً عامة كلية بالنسبة إليها، وكان مستندهم ما أجروه من استقراء ناقص حولها. وظاهر أن الاستقراء الناقص الصحيح قد يفيد الظن بالحكم الكلي، ولكنه لا يفيد اليقين به، لاحتمال أن يكون ما لم يدرس على خلاف ما درس، ولجواز أن يظهر في المستقبل خطأ الحكم الكلي المستند إلى القياس الناقص. وتاريخ العلوم المادية مشحون بمثل هذه الأخطاء العلمية الناتجة عن إصدار أحكام كلية عامة، بالاستناد إلى حالات خاصة، واستقراءات ناقصات، ولذلك يحتاج التقدم العلمي إلى أن يعدّل من المعارف السابقة كلما تكشف له جديد في مجال المعرفة الاستقرائية، وعلى الرغم من كل ذلك فقد انتفع الناس انتفاعاً كبيراً من منجزات حضارية عظيمة، عن طريق اتخاذ الاستقراء الناقص مبدأ لتحصيل معارف كلية. وقد دفع الاستقراء الناقص العلماء إلى البحث في كل مجالات المعرفة، وأخذ بيد العلم إلى تطور جليل، وتقدم مطرد، ولذلك أطلق عليه علماء النهضة العلمية المادية الحديثة اسم الاستقراء العلمي. الاستقراء الناقص رياضي وعلمي: ويقسمون الاستقراء الناقص إلى قسمين: رياضي وعلمي. أما الاستقراء الرياضي: فهو الذي يتم في مجال العلوم الرياضية، ويكفي فيه الاعتماد على مثال واحد أو عدة أمثلة، لاستخراج قاعدة كلية أو قانون عام، لأن الفكر يستطيع الحكم بسرعة على كل ما لم يتصفحه من جزئيات، حين يصل إلى قاعدة رياضية من خلال مثال واحد أو عدة أمثلة. وأما الاستقراء العلمي: فهو الذي يتم عادة في مجال العلوم الطبيعية والكيميائية والاجتماعية والاقتصادية ونحوها، ونسبة التأكد فيه أضعف من نسبة التأكد التي تحصل في الاستقراء الرياضي، لأن احتمالات مخالفة الواقع لما دل عليه الاستقراء الناقص هنا أقوى من نظيراتها في الاستقراء الرياضي.
The remaining parts or particulars are measured against it, and based on that the researcher issues a probable general judgment covering what he studied and what he did not study, based on his preponderant belief that the system of the universe has universal laws in which abnormality is rare as long as the studied type is one, or based on his preponderant belief that the remaining parts are similar to the parts he studied. When one wants to know the original and foreign elements in human blood, a small quantity is studied from it, then the analyzing doctor gives his judgment on all the blood based on the quantity he studied. And when one wants to know the type of agricultural soil and its elements and characteristics, samples are taken from multiple places, and laboratory analysis and examination are performed on them, then the analyzing engineer issues his judgment on all the soil of this land based on the samples he studied. Most of the natural laws that natural researchers have arrived at, they only arrived at them through incomplete induction. People from ancient times have had general universal judgments, relying in them on the results they arrived at through incomplete induction, and these judgments were useful and beneficial. Experiments clarified to them that poison is lethal, so they issued a general judgment on poison that it is lethal, even though their induction was incomplete and not perfect, but they measured what they did not examine against what they examined, by virtue of the similarity between the studied part and the unstudied part. Experiments clarified to them that some drugs are laxatives, some are astringents, some are beneficial for the liver, some are strengthening for nerves, and so on, so they issued general universal judgments regarding them, and their basis was what they conducted of incomplete induction about them. It is apparent that correct incomplete induction may yield probability (zann) of the universal judgment, but it does not yield certainty (yaqin) about it, due to the possibility that what was not studied may be contrary to what was studied, and because it is possible that in the future the error of the universal judgment based on incomplete inference (al-qiyas al-naqis) may appear. The history of material sciences is full of such scientific errors resulting from issuing general universal judgments based on particular cases and incomplete inductions, and therefore scientific progress needs to modify previous knowledge whenever new things are revealed to it in the field of inductive knowledge. Despite all that, people have benefited greatly from great civilizational achievements by adopting incomplete induction as a principle for acquiring universal knowledge. Incomplete induction has driven scientists to research in all fields of knowledge, and has taken science to great development and steady progress, and therefore the modern material scientific renaissance scholars called it scientific induction. Incomplete induction is mathematical and scientific: They divide incomplete induction into two parts: mathematical and scientific. As for mathematical induction: it is that which occurs in the field of mathematical sciences, and it suffices to rely on one example or several examples to extract a universal rule or general law, because the mind can quickly judge all the particulars it has not examined when it arrives at a mathematical rule through one example or several examples. As for scientific induction: it is that which usually occurs in the fields of natural sciences, chemistry, social sciences, economics, and the like, and the degree of certainty in it is weaker than the degree of certainty obtained in mathematical induction, because the probabilities that reality contradicts what the incomplete induction indicated are stronger here than their counterparts in mathematical induction.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of incomplete induction (al-istiqra' al-naqis). The author begins by defining it as a method where a researcher studies a part of a class and then issues a probable general judgment about the whole, based on the assumption that the universe operates under uniform laws or that unexamined parts resemble examined ones. Concrete examples are given: a doctor analyzing a blood sample to judge the entire blood, and an engineer testing soil samples to characterize all the soil. The author notes that most natural laws have been discovered through incomplete induction, and that people have long relied on it for useful general judgments, such as the lethality of poison or the properties of drugs. The epistemic status is then clarified: incomplete induction yields probability (zann) but not certainty (yaqin), because the unstudied may differ or future discoveries may show error. The history of science is filled with such errors, yet incomplete induction remains essential for scientific progress and has been called 'scientific induction' by modern scholars. Finally, the author classifies incomplete induction into two types: mathematical induction, used in mathematics where a single example can yield a universal rule with high certainty, and scientific induction, used in empirical sciences where certainty is weaker due to higher chances of exceptions. The passage highlights the utility and limitations of inductive reasoning.

الظن
اليقين

Epistemic Weight and Types of Incomplete Induction
Author discusses epistemic status and classification of incomplete induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why incomplete induction yields zann not yaqin, and distinguish between mathematical and scientific induction.

Core Idea
Certainty belongs to deduction; induction yields only probability.

The passage not only defines incomplete induction but also carefully evaluates its epistemic status. It then divides the method into two subtypes: mathematical induction (with higher certainty) and scientific induction (with lower certainty). This lesson focuses on that critical distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Locate the epistemic claim
Find the sentence: 'ظاهر أن الاستقراء الناقص الصحيح قد يفيد الظن بالحكم الكلي، ولكنه لا يفيد اليقين به'. This is the core epistemic statement. Note the contrast between zann and yaqin.
The author gives two reasons: the unstudied may differ, and future errors may appear.
2
Identify the two types
Read the final paragraph starting with 'الاستقراء الناقص رياضي وعلمي'. The author divides incomplete induction into mathematical and scientific. Highlight the criteria for each.
Mathematical: one example suffices, high certainty. Scientific: multiple examples needed, lower certainty.
3
Compare certainty levels
Why does mathematical induction yield higher certainty? The author says because the mind can quickly judge all particulars once it grasps the rule. In empirical sciences, exceptions are more likely.
This is not modern mathematical induction; it is a form of inductive generalization in mathematics.
4
Apply to Islamic sciences
In usul al-fiqh, most rulings are zanni (probable) because they are derived through inductive reasoning from the texts. Certainty (qat'i) is rare. This mirrors the author's point about incomplete induction yielding zann.
For example, the prohibition of riba is derived inductively from specific verses and hadiths; it is considered qat'i by consensus, but many other rulings are zanni.

أن يكون ما لم يدرس على خلاف ما درس، ولجواز أن يظهر في المستقبل خطأ الحكم الكلي المستند إلى القياس الناقص. وتاريخ العلوم المادية مشحون بمثل هذه الأخطاء العلمية الناتجة عن إصدار أحكام كلية عامة، بالاستناد إلى حالات خاصة، واستقراءات ناقصات، ولذلك يحتاج التقدم العلمي إلى أن يعدّل من المعارف السابقة كلما تكشف له جديد في مجال المعرفة الاستقرائية، وعلى الرغم من كل ذلك فقد انتفع الناس انتفاعاً كبيراً من منجزات حضارية عظيمة، عن طريق اتخاذ الاستقراء الناقص مبدأ لتحصيل معارف كلية. وقد دفع الاستقراء الناقص العلماء إلى البحث في كل مجالات المعرفة، وأخذ بيد العلم إلى تطور جليل، وتقدم مطرد، ولذلك أطلق عليه علماء النهضة العلمية المادية الحديثة اسم الاستقراء العلمي. الاستقراء الناقص رياضي وعلمي: ويقسمون الاستقراء الناقص إلى قسمين: رياضي وعلمي. أما الاستقراء الرياضي: فهو الذي يتم في مجال العلوم الرياضية، ويكفي فيه الاعتماد على مثال واحد أو عدة أمثلة، لاستخراج قاعدة كلية أو قانون عام، لأن الفكر يستطيع الحكم بسرعة على كل ما لم يتصفحه من جزئيات، حين يصل إلى قاعدة رياضية من خلال مثال واحد أو عدة أمثلة. وأما الاستقراء العلمي: فهو الذي يتم عادة في مجال العلوم الطبيعية والكيميائية والاجتماعية والاقتصادية ونحوها، ونسبة التأكد فيه أضعف من نسبة التأكد التي تحصل في الاستقراء الرياضي، لأن احتمالات مخالفة الواقع لما دل عليه الاستقراء الناقص هنا أقوى من نظيراتها في الاستقراء الرياضي.
The remaining parts or particulars are measured against it, and based on that the researcher issues a probable general judgment covering what he studied and what he did not study, based on his preponderant belief that the system of the universe has universal laws in which abnormality is rare as long as the studied type is one, or based on his preponderant belief that the remaining parts are similar to the parts he studied. When one wants to know the original and foreign elements in human blood, a small quantity is studied from it, then the analyzing doctor gives his judgment on all the blood based on the quantity he studied. And when one wants to know the type of agricultural soil and its elements and characteristics, samples are taken from multiple places, and laboratory analysis and examination are performed on them, then the analyzing engineer issues his judgment on all the soil of this land based on the samples he studied. Most of the natural laws that natural researchers have arrived at, they only arrived at them through incomplete induction. People from ancient times have had general universal judgments, relying in them on the results they arrived at through incomplete induction, and these judgments were useful and beneficial. Experiments clarified to them that poison is lethal, so they issued a general judgment on poison that it is lethal, even though their induction was incomplete and not perfect, but they measured what they did not examine against what they examined, by virtue of the similarity between the studied part and the unstudied part. Experiments clarified to them that some drugs are laxatives, some are astringents, some are beneficial for the liver, some are strengthening for nerves, and so on, so they issued general universal judgments regarding them, and their basis was what they conducted of incomplete induction about them. It is apparent that correct incomplete induction may yield probability (zann) of the universal judgment, but it does not yield certainty (yaqin) about it, due to the possibility that what was not studied may be contrary to what was studied, and because it is possible that in the future the error of the universal judgment based on incomplete inference (al-qiyas al-naqis) may appear. The history of material sciences is full of such scientific errors resulting from issuing general universal judgments based on particular cases and incomplete inductions, and therefore scientific progress needs to modify previous knowledge whenever new things are revealed to it in the field of inductive knowledge. Despite all that, people have benefited greatly from great civilizational achievements by adopting incomplete induction as a principle for acquiring universal knowledge. Incomplete induction has driven scientists to research in all fields of knowledge, and has taken science to great development and steady progress, and therefore the modern material scientific renaissance scholars called it scientific induction. Incomplete induction is mathematical and scientific: They divide incomplete induction into two parts: mathematical and scientific. As for mathematical induction: it is that which occurs in the field of mathematical sciences, and it suffices to rely on one example or several examples to extract a universal rule or general law, because the mind can quickly judge all the particulars it has not examined when it arrives at a mathematical rule through one example or several examples. As for scientific induction: it is that which usually occurs in the fields of natural sciences, chemistry, social sciences, economics, and the like, and the degree of certainty in it is weaker than the degree of certainty obtained in mathematical induction, because the probabilities that reality contradicts what the incomplete induction indicated are stronger here than their counterparts in mathematical induction.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of incomplete induction (al-istiqra' al-naqis). The author begins by defining it as a method where a researcher studies a part of a class and then issues a probable general judgment about the whole, based on the assumption that the universe operates under uniform laws or that unexamined parts resemble examined ones. Concrete examples are given: a doctor analyzing a blood sample to judge the entire blood, and an engineer testing soil samples to characterize all the soil. The author notes that most natural laws have been discovered through incomplete induction, and that people have long relied on it for useful general judgments, such as the lethality of poison or the properties of drugs. The epistemic status is then clarified: incomplete induction yields probability (zann) but not certainty (yaqin), because the unstudied may differ or future discoveries may show error. The history of science is filled with such errors, yet incomplete induction remains essential for scientific progress and has been called 'scientific induction' by modern scholars. Finally, the author classifies incomplete induction into two types: mathematical induction, used in mathematics where a single example can yield a universal rule with high certainty, and scientific induction, used in empirical sciences where certainty is weaker due to higher chances of exceptions. The passage highlights the utility and limitations of inductive reasoning.

اليقين

الاستقراء العلمي

Incomplete Induction: Probability, Not Certainty
Author explains why incomplete induction yields probability, not certainty, and its role in science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the epistemological limitation of incomplete induction and explain why it yields only probability (zann) rather than certainty (yaqin).

Core Idea
Incomplete induction gives useful probability, never absolute certainty.

This passage explains a crucial epistemological point: even a correct incomplete induction cannot produce certain knowledge (yaqin). The author shows that the very nature of incomplete induction leaves room for error, yet he also acknowledges its immense practical value in driving scientific progress. This lesson will help you understand the logical basis for this limitation and see how it applies to real inductive arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about incomplete induction
The author asserts that correct incomplete induction may yield probability (zann) of a universal judgment, but not certainty (yaqin). This is the central thesis.
The term 'zann' in Islamic epistemology is contrasted with 'yaqin'; it is sufficient for action but not for absolute conviction.
2
Locate the two reasons for uncertainty
Find in the Arabic: 'لاحتمال أن يكون ما لم يدرس على خلاف ما درس، ولجواز أن يظهر في المستقبل خطأ الحكم الكلي'. These two possibilities—unexamined instances may differ and future evidence may falsify—prevent certainty.
3
Analyze the example from scientific history
The author references errors in material sciences due to hasty generalizations. How does this illustrate the risk? Even successful theories are subject to revision when new evidence emerges.
In Islamic sciences, similar revisions occur in fiqh when new cases challenge previous inductive generalizations.
4
Recognize the practical value despite uncertainty
The author balances limitation with utility: incomplete induction has driven great discoveries and civilizational achievements. Why do scientists still use it? Because it yields sufficiently reliable knowledge for progress, though not certainty.
This reflects the principle in usul al-fiqh that zann can be a valid basis for rulings when no certainty is attainable.
5
Distinguish between zann and yaqin in Islamic epistemology
Understand how this distinction matters: in fiqh, zann can ground a ruling (e.g., analogical deduction), while in kalam, yaqin is required for matters of 'aqida. The passage's analysis applies directly to these fields.
For example, the 'illa (effective cause) derived via incomplete induction is zanni unless explicitly confirmed by text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وظاهر أن الاستقراء الناقص الصحيح قد يفيد الظن بالحكم الكلي، ولكنه لا يفيد اليقين به، لاحتمال أن يكون ما لم يدرس على خلاف ما درس، ولجواز أن يظهر في المستقبل خطأ الحكم الكلي المستند إلى القياس الناقص . وتاريخ العلوم المادية مشحون بمثل هذه الأخطاء العلمية الناتجة عن إصدار أحكام كلية عامة، بالاستناد إلى حالات خاصة، واستقراءات ناقصات، ولذلك يحتاج التقدم العلمي إلى أن يعدّل من المعارف السابقة كلما تكشف له جديد في مجال المعرفة الاستقرائية، وعلى الرغم من كل ذلك فقد انتفع الناس انتفاعاً كبيراً من منجزات حضارية عظيمة، عن طريق اتخاذ الاستقراء الناقص مبدأ لتحصيل معارف كلية . وقد دفع الاستقراء الناقص العلماء إلى البحث في كل مجالات المعرفة، وأخذ بيد العلم إلى تطور جليل، وتقدم مطرد، ولذلك أطلق عليه علماء النهضة العلمية المادية الحديثة اسم الاستقراء العلمي .
It is apparent that correct incomplete induction may yield probability of a universal judgment, but it does not yield certainty about it, due to the possibility that what has not been studied may contradict what was studied, and due to the possibility that in the future the universal judgment based on incomplete induction may appear erroneous. The history of the material sciences is filled with such scientific errors resulting from issuing general universal judgments based on particular cases and incomplete inductions. Therefore, scientific progress needs to modify previous knowledge whenever new things are revealed in the field of inductive knowledge. Despite all that, people have greatly benefited from great civilizational achievements by adopting incomplete induction as a principle for acquiring universal knowledge. Incomplete induction drove scientists to research in all fields of knowledge and led science to great development and steady progress, which is why the scholars of the modern material scientific renaissance called it scientific induction.

Commentary
This passage discusses the epistemological status of incomplete induction (al-istiqra' al-naqis). The author begins by stating a key epistemological limitation: even a correct incomplete induction can at most yield probability (zann) regarding a universal judgment, not certainty (yaqin). This is because any instance not yet examined could contradict the pattern observed, and it is always possible that future evidence will falsify the generalization. The author then illustrates this with reference to the history of the material sciences, which are replete with errors that arose from jumping to universal conclusions based on limited cases. Consequently, scientific progress requires constant revision of prior knowledge as new data emerges from inductive inquiry. Despite this limitation, incomplete induction has been immensely productive; it has enabled civilizations to achieve great advances by using it as a principle to acquire universal knowledge. In fact, modern material science has relied so heavily on it that scholars of the scientific renaissance have called it 'scientific induction' (al-istiqra' al-'ilmi). The author thus affirms both the practical value and the epistemic limitation of incomplete induction.

Division of Incomplete Induction: Mathematical vs Scientific
Author classifies incomplete induction into mathematical and scientific types, describing each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mathematical and scientific induction and explain their respective degrees of certainty.

Core Idea
Mathematical induction yields high certainty; scientific induction is weaker but indispensable for empirical sciences.

In this passage, the author divides incomplete induction into two categories: mathematical and scientific. Understanding this division is crucial for recognizing the reliability of inductive arguments in different fields. Mathematical induction, common in abstract sciences, allows quick generalization from few examples, while scientific induction, used in empirical sciences, carries less certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of incomplete induction
Read the first sentence: 'Incomplete induction is mathematical and scientific.' The author explicitly names the two divisions.
2
Extract the characteristics of mathematical induction
Note that mathematical induction occurs in mathematical sciences, relies on one or few examples, and yields a universal rule quickly. The mind can judge unexamined particulars with certainty.
3
Extract the characteristics of scientific induction
Scientific induction occurs in natural, chemical, social, economic sciences. Its certainty is weaker because the likelihood of counterexamples is higher.
4
Compare the certainty levels
The author states that the degree of certainty in scientific induction is 'weaker' than in mathematical induction. Contrast the reasons: abstract vs empirical domains.
5
Identify the author's conclusion about scientific induction
Despite its weakness, scientific induction is necessary for progress in those sciences, unless stronger sources (like definitive religious data) are available.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستقراء الناقص رياضي وعلمي : ويقسمون الاستقراء الناقص إلى قسمين : رياضي وعلمي . أما الاستقراء الرياضي : فهو الذي يتم في مجال العلوم الرياضية، ويكفي فيه الاعتماد على مثال واحد أو عدة أمثلة، لاستخراج قاعدة كلية أو قانون عام، لأن الفكر يستطيع الحكم بسرعة على كل ما لم يتصفحه من جزئيات، حين يصل إلى قاعدة رياضية من خلال مثال واحد أو عدة أمثلة . وأما الاستقراء العلمي : فهو الذي يتم عادة في مجال العلوم الطبيعية والكيميائية والاجتماعية والاقتصادية ونحوها، ونسبة التأكد فيه أضعف من نسبة التأكد التي تحصل في الاستقراء الرياضي، لأن احتمالات مخالفة الواقع لما دل عليه الاستقراء الناقص هنا أقوى من نظيراتها في الاستقراء الرياضي . ومع ذلك فلا مندوحة من الاعتماد عليه في مجال هذه العلوم، لأنه الوسيلة الوحيدة لتقدمها، ما لم يكن لدى الإنسان طريق أقوى من الاستقراء الناقص وأقوم منهجاً، كالبيانات الدينية الصحيحة ذات الدلالات القطعية.
Incomplete induction is mathematical and scientific. They divide incomplete induction into two types: mathematical and scientific. As for mathematical induction, it takes place in the field of mathematical sciences, and it suffices to rely on one or several examples to extract a universal rule or general law, because the mind can quickly judge all the particulars it has not examined once it reaches a mathematical rule from one or several examples. As for scientific induction, it usually takes place in the field of natural, chemical, social, economic sciences and the like, and its degree of certainty is weaker than the degree of certainty obtained in mathematical induction, because the possibilities of reality contradicting what incomplete induction indicates are stronger here than their counterparts in mathematical induction. Nevertheless, there is no escape from relying on it in the field of these sciences, because it is the only means for their progress, unless one has a stronger path than incomplete induction and a more upright methodology, such as correct religious data with definitive indications.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage classifies incomplete induction into two types: mathematical and scientific. The author first states the division: incomplete induction is either mathematical (riyāḍī) or scientific ('ilmī). Mathematical induction occurs in mathematical sciences; it is sufficient to rely on just one or a few examples to derive a universal rule, because the mind quickly grasps the general principle from limited instances. The degree of certainty in mathematical induction is high because mathematical objects are abstract and regular. In contrast, scientific induction occurs in natural, chemical, social, and economic sciences. Its certainty is weaker because the likelihood of encountering counterexamples is greater. Despite this, scientific induction is indispensable for the progress of these fields; there is no alternative as reliable unless one possesses stronger sources like definitive religious data (al-bayānāt al-dīniyya al-ṣaḥīḥa dhāt al-dalālāt al-qaṭ'iyya). The author implies that in matters not covered by revelation, scientific induction remains the only viable method.

Key Terms
الاستقراء الرياضي
الاستقراء العلمي

Key Takeaway
Incomplete induction is divided into mathematical (high certainty) and scientific (weaker certainty), but scientific induction is necessary for empirical Islamic sciences unless definitive revelation is available.

The Law of Causality as the Basis for Incomplete Induction
Author presents causality as a law justifying incomplete induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the law of causality, as both an empirical and rational principle, justifies incomplete induction.

Core Idea
The law of causality transforms repeated observations into a universal principle that underlies all inductive reasoning.

In this passage, the author introduces the first of two laws that give incomplete induction its logical force: the law of causality. He argues that this law is not merely a generalization from experience but also has a rational foundation that makes it universally reliable. We will explore his reasoning and examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's purpose
Read the opening sentence: the author is providing the 'logical support' (al-sanad al-manṭiqī) for incomplete induction. Recognize that causality is one of two laws governing natural phenomena.
2
Locate the definition of the law of causality
Find the phrase 'qānūn al-ʿilliyya' in the Arabic. The author defines it as every event having a cause (كل حادثة ... أثر لسبب).
3
Examine the examples provided
List the examples: clouds from evaporation, rain from condensation, tree from seed, explosion from a cause, mercury expansion from heat. Note they are all natural phenomena from everyday experience.
These examples show the law’s empirical basis.
4
Distinguish the two bases for the law
The author states the law is derived from incomplete induction (empirical) yet also says the connection between an event and a cause is a pure rational principle (أصل عقلي صرف). Identify this dual basis.
The rational aspect makes the law inviolable in principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسند المنطقي الذي يدعو إلى الأخذ بهذا الاستقراء الناقص في مجال العلوم الطبيعية والاجتماعية والاقتصادية وغيرها، يرجع إلى ملاحظة قانونين مهيمنين على ظواهر الطبيعة بصفة عامة، هما: قانون العلية (أي: السببية)، وقانون الاطراد. أما قانون العلية (أي: السببية): فهو القانون المستفاد من تجارب الإنسان الكثيرة، وخبراته التي لا تحصى، إذ كشفت له هذه الخبرات والتجارب أن كل حادثة في الكون وكل ظاهرة من ظواهره، إنما هي أثر لسبب من الأسباب أو علة من العلل. فتتكون السحب بسبب تبخر المياه في الأرض، وتنزل الأمطار بسبب تكاثف ذرات البخار الناتج عن مرور موجة رياح باردة في مستوى السحاب. وتنبت الشجرة بسبب وجود البذرة في الأرض الملائمة تربة وسقياً. ويحصل انفجار في مكان ما فيجزم الناس جميعاً بأن سبباً ما قد أحدث هذا الانفجار. ويتمدد الزئبق في ميزان الحرارة فنعلم أن السبب في ذلك هو ارتفاع درجة الحرارة. صحيح أن هذا القانون مستفاد من الاستقراء الناقص، إلا أنه لم يخرم في أية تجربة من تجارب الإنسان، وفي أية ظاهرة من ظواهر الكون، الأمر الذي جعله أحد القوانين الكونية المسلم بها. يضاف إلى ذلك أن أصل ارتباط الحدوث بسبب سابق عليه أصل عقلي صرف، لا مجال للتشكك فيه. وأما قانون الاطراد: فهو قانون مستفاد أيضاً من تجارب الإنسان الكثيرة وخبراته التي لا تحصى، وخلاصته: أن الأسباب والعلل المتشابهة تنتج عنها آثار متشابهة.
And the logical support that calls for adopting this incomplete induction in the field of natural, social, economic and other sciences goes back to observing two laws governing natural phenomena in general: the law of causality (i.e., causation) and the law of uniformity. As for the law of causality (i.e., causation), it is the law derived from man's many experiments and countless experiences, for these experiences and experiments have revealed to him that every event in the universe and every phenomenon of its phenomena is but an effect of some cause or some reason. Thus, clouds form due to the evaporation of water on earth, and rain falls due to the condensation of vapor particles resulting from the passage of a cold wind wave at the cloud level. The tree grows due to the presence of the seed in soil suitable in terms of composition and irrigation. An explosion occurs somewhere, and all people are certain that some cause produced that explosion. Mercury expands in the thermometer, and we know that the cause of that is the rise in temperature. True, this law is derived from incomplete induction, yet it has never been violated in any of man's experiments or in any phenomenon of the universe, which has made it one of the accepted universal laws. Added to that, the principle of linking an occurrence to a prior cause is a pure rational principle, leaving no room for doubt. As for the law of uniformity, it is also a law derived from man's many experiments and countless experiences, and its summary is that similar causes and reasons produce similar effects.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage provides the logical basis (al-sanad al-manṭiqī) for relying on incomplete induction in the natural, social, and economic sciences. The author identifies two fundamental laws: the law of causality (qānūn al-'illiyya) and the law of uniformity (qānūn al-iṭṭirād). Causality means that every event or phenomenon has a cause. The author illustrates this with everyday examples: clouds from evaporation, rain from condensation, tree growth from seed, explosions from a cause, mercury expansion from heat. This law is derived from numerous human experiences via incomplete induction, yet it has never been falsified, making it a universally accepted law. Furthermore, the author adds that the connection between an occurrence and a prior cause is a purely rational principle (aṣl 'aqlī ṣirf), not merely empirical. The second law, uniformity, states that similar causes produce similar effects. This law is also derived from countless experiences. Together, these two laws provide the justification for inductive reasoning: because we observe regular cause-effect relationships and assume uniformity, we can generalize from observed cases to unobserved ones.

Key Terms
قانون العلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It has never been violated in any experiment and is also a rational principle.
C) It is based on the authority of ancient philosophers.
D) It applies only to social sciences, not natural sciences.

Key Takeaway
The law of causality is both empirically unbroken and rationally necessary, making it a secure logical support for incomplete induction.

The Law of Uniformity as the Basis for Incomplete Induction
Author presents uniformity as the second law justifying incomplete induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the law of uniformity, inferred from repeated experience, supports the generalization from observed cases to unobserved cases in incomplete induction.

Core Idea
The law of uniformity ensures that similar causes produce similar effects, allowing past observations to predict future outcomes.

Having discussed causality, the author now turns to the second law underpinning incomplete induction: the law of uniformity (qānūn al-iṭṭirād). This law states that similar causes yield similar effects. Like causality, it is derived from countless experiences and serves as a psychological and logical basis for expecting the future to resemble the past.

Guided Path
1
Identify the law of uniformity in the text
Find the phrase 'qānūn al-iṭṭirād' in the Arabic. The author introduces it after causality.
2
Extract its definition
The author states: 'الأسباب والعلل المتشابهة تنتج عنها آثار متشابهة' (similar causes and reasons produce similar effects). Write this in your own words.
3
Understand its derivation
The law is said to be derived from man's many experiments and countless experiences (مستفاد من تجارب الإنسان الكثيرة وخبراته التي لا تحصى). Note that no specific examples are given here—unlike causality.
The law is summarized in one sentence; the author does not elaborate further in this passage.
4
Connect to incomplete induction
Consider how uniformity justifies induction: if we observe that a specific cause (e.g., heat) always produces a specific effect (e.g., expansion of mercury) in many instances, we infer that it will do so in all similar instances because of uniformity. This is the logical leap from 'some' to 'all'.
Uniformity is the assumption that nature is consistent across time and space.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسند المنطقي الذي يدعو إلى الأخذ بهذا الاستقراء الناقص في مجال العلوم الطبيعية والاجتماعية والاقتصادية وغيرها، يرجع إلى ملاحظة قانونين مهيمنين على ظواهر الطبيعة بصفة عامة، هما: قانون العلية (أي: السببية)، وقانون الاطراد. أما قانون العلية (أي: السببية): فهو القانون المستفاد من تجارب الإنسان الكثيرة، وخبراته التي لا تحصى، إذ كشفت له هذه الخبرات والتجارب أن كل حادثة في الكون وكل ظاهرة من ظواهره، إنما هي أثر لسبب من الأسباب أو علة من العلل. فتتكون السحب بسبب تبخر المياه في الأرض، وتنزل الأمطار بسبب تكاثف ذرات البخار الناتج عن مرور موجة رياح باردة في مستوى السحاب. وتنبت الشجرة بسبب وجود البذرة في الأرض الملائمة تربة وسقياً. ويحصل انفجار في مكان ما فيجزم الناس جميعاً بأن سبباً ما قد أحدث هذا الانفجار. ويتمدد الزئبق في ميزان الحرارة فنعلم أن السبب في ذلك هو ارتفاع درجة الحرارة. صحيح أن هذا القانون مستفاد من الاستقراء الناقص، إلا أنه لم يخرم في أية تجربة من تجارب الإنسان، وفي أية ظاهرة من ظواهر الكون، الأمر الذي جعله أحد القوانين الكونية المسلم بها. يضاف إلى ذلك أن أصل ارتباط الحدوث بسبب سابق عليه أصل عقلي صرف، لا مجال للتشكك فيه. وأما قانون الاطراد: فهو قانون مستفاد أيضاً من تجارب الإنسان الكثيرة وخبراته التي لا تحصى، وخلاصته: أن الأسباب والعلل المتشابهة تنتج عنها آثار متشابهة.
And the logical support that calls for adopting this incomplete induction in the field of natural, social, economic and other sciences goes back to observing two laws governing natural phenomena in general: the law of causality (i.e., causation) and the law of uniformity. As for the law of causality (i.e., causation), it is the law derived from man's many experiments and countless experiences, for these experiences and experiments have revealed to him that every event in the universe and every phenomenon of its phenomena is but an effect of some cause or some reason. Thus, clouds form due to the evaporation of water on earth, and rain falls due to the condensation of vapor particles resulting from the passage of a cold wind wave at the cloud level. The tree grows due to the presence of the seed in soil suitable in terms of composition and irrigation. An explosion occurs somewhere, and all people are certain that some cause produced that explosion. Mercury expands in the thermometer, and we know that the cause of that is the rise in temperature. True, this law is derived from incomplete induction, yet it has never been violated in any of man's experiments or in any phenomenon of the universe, which has made it one of the accepted universal laws. Added to that, the principle of linking an occurrence to a prior cause is a pure rational principle, leaving no room for doubt. As for the law of uniformity, it is also a law derived from man's many experiments and countless experiences, and its summary is that similar causes and reasons produce similar effects.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage provides the logical basis (al-sanad al-manṭiqī) for relying on incomplete induction in the natural, social, and economic sciences. The author identifies two fundamental laws: the law of causality (qānūn al-'illiyya) and the law of uniformity (qānūn al-iṭṭirād). Causality means that every event or phenomenon has a cause. The author illustrates this with everyday examples: clouds from evaporation, rain from condensation, tree growth from seed, explosions from a cause, mercury expansion from heat. This law is derived from numerous human experiences via incomplete induction, yet it has never been falsified, making it a universally accepted law. Furthermore, the author adds that the connection between an occurrence and a prior cause is a purely rational principle (aṣl 'aqlī ṣirf), not merely empirical. The second law, uniformity, states that similar causes produce similar effects. This law is also derived from countless experiences. Together, these two laws provide the justification for inductive reasoning: because we observe regular cause-effect relationships and assume uniformity, we can generalize from observed cases to unobserved ones.

Key Terms
قانون الاطراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Similar causes produce similar effects.
C) All effects are unique and non-repeating.
D) Uniformity is only valid in mathematics.

Key Takeaway
The law of uniformity justifies the leap from observed to unobserved cases by asserting that similar causes will consistently yield similar effects.

Induction and Analogy

The Justification of Incomplete Induction
Explains the epistemological basis for incomplete induction via causality and uniformity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how incomplete induction is justified in empirical sciences through the laws of causality and uniformity, and cite examples from the passage.

Core Idea
Incomplete induction is validated by two universal laws: causality and uniformity, which are both empirically and rationally grounded.

In this passage, the author argues that incomplete induction is the primary method for gaining knowledge in the natural and social sciences, unless one has access to stronger sources like definitive religious data. The logical basis for relying on incomplete induction rests on two fundamental laws: the law of causality and the law of uniformity. We will explore how these laws are derived from experience and reason, and how they make inductive generalization reliable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being justified
Find the statement that incomplete induction is the only means of progress in empirical sciences. Note the exception of 'correct religious data with definitive indications.'
2
Extract the two laws
Locate 'qanun al-'illiyya (al-sababiyya)' and 'qanun al-ithtiraad' in the Arabic text. Understand that they are presented as the logical basis for induction.
3
Examine examples for the law of causality
List the examples: cloud formation, rain, plant growth, explosion, mercury expansion. Notice how each demonstrates a cause-effect relationship.
4
Examine examples for the law of uniformity
List the examples: throat-cutting causing death, eating causing satiety, fire consuming living beings. Note that exceptions are explained by intervening causes.
5
Understand the rational basis
The author states that the connection between an occurrence and its cause is a purely rational principle with no room for doubt. This elevates causality from empirical to rational certainty.

الاطراد: فهو قانون مستفاد أيضاً من تجارب الإنسان الكثيرة وخبراته التي لا تحصى، وخلاصته: أن الأسباب والعلل المتشابهة تنتج عنها مسببات ومعلولات متشابهة. فكلما وجد قطع الرقبة في مستمر العادة تسبب عنه موت صاحب الرقبة إذا كان حياً. وكلما جاع الإنسان فأكل وملأ بطنه تسبب عنه في مستمر العادة حالة الشبع، وعدم الشعور بالحاجة إلى الطعام، وفي حالة عدم شعوره بالشبع رغم الأكل الكثير الذي أكله، نحيل الأمر إلى سبب مرضي أفسد حالته الطبيعية. وكلما قذف حيٌّ في النار أكلته النار فمات ضمن قانون اطراد الآثار السببية التي تحدثها النار، وحينما لا تأكله النار وينجو منها في حالة من الحالات، فإننا نحيل الأمر إلى وجود سبب آخر عطل تأثير النار، ولا بد أن يكون هذا السبب الآخر مبرداً لحرارة النار أو واقياً للجسم من حرها، على أن الأمر لا يخلو من سبب، والمعجزات ترجع إلى سبب الخلق الرباني المباشر، أو إلى خلقه بأسباب غير خاضعة لسلطة القدرات الإنسانية. وضمن قانون الاطراد يقوم الناس بأعمالهم وصناعاتهم وكل شؤون حياتهم، وهم يعتقدون أن الأسباب ستؤدي إلى النتائج المعروفة لها، وحين يحدث خلل في النتيجة يرجع الإنسان فيحاسب نفسه عن السبب، ويعيد النظر في عمله ليكتشف مواقع الخطأ في السبب الذي قام به. وفي هيمنة قانوني السببية والاطراد، استطاع البحث العلمي أن يعتمد على الاستقراء الناقص في استخراج قواعد كلية وقوانين طبيعية عامة، ولكن قد يأتي خطأ البحث العلمي من تحديد السبب أو من تحديد العلة، فقد يكون السبب أو العلة غير الذي ظهر للباحث، ويأتي خطأ الباحث من مشاهدته لعنصر مرافق للسبب الحقيقي، فيحكم عليه بأنه هو السبب، أو من مشاهدته لبعض عناصر السبب فيحكم عليها بأنها هي السبب كله، وهكذا إلى عدة عوامل تجعله يخطئ في الحكم التعميمي الذي يصدره. وليست كل الأحكام الاستقرائية تستند إلى قانوني العلية والاطراد، فبعض الأحكام تستند إلى قانون الاطراد الاتفاقي فقط، دون أن يرجع هذا الاطراد إلى سبب يعلل به حدوث الحالات المطردة.
The only means of its progress, unless a person has a way stronger than incomplete induction and a more upright methodology, like the correct religious data with definitive indications. And the logical basis that calls for adopting this incomplete induction in the field of natural, social, economic, and other sciences, goes back to the observation of two laws dominant over the phenomena of nature in general: they are: the law of causality (i.e., causation), and the law of uniformity. As for the law of causality (i.e., causation): it is the law derived from man's many experiences and his countless experiences, for these experiences and experiments have revealed to him that every event in the universe and every phenomenon among its phenomena is but an effect of some cause or a cause among causes. Thus clouds form due to evaporation of water on earth, and rain falls due to condensation of vapor particles resulting from the passage of a cold wave at the cloud level. And the tree grows due to the presence of the seed in suitable soil and watering. And an explosion occurs somewhere, so all people are certain that some cause has caused this explosion. And the mercury expands in the thermometer, so we know that the reason for that is the rise in temperature. It is true that this law is derived from incomplete induction, but it has not been violated in any experiment of man, nor in any phenomenon of the universe, which made it one of the universally accepted cosmic laws. Added to that is that the origin of the connection of an occurrence to a preceding cause is a purely rational origin, with no room for doubt. And as for the law of uniformity: it is a law also derived from man's many experiences and his countless experiences, and its summary: that similar causes and 'illas produce similar effects and outcomes. So whenever cutting of the neck occurs in the continuous custom, it causes the death of the neck's owner if he is alive. And whenever a person becomes hungry, eats, and fills his stomach, it usually causes the state of satiety and the absence of feeling the need for food, and in the case of his not feeling satiety despite the much food he ate, we refer the matter to a pathological cause that corrupted his natural state. And whenever a living being is thrown into fire, the fire consumes it and it dies within the law of uniformity of the causal effects that fire produces, and when the fire does not consume it and it escapes from it in some case, we refer the matter to the existence of another cause that neutralized the effect of fire, and that other cause must be a cooler of the fire's heat or a protector of the body from its heat, and the matter is not without a cause, and miracles are referred to the cause of direct divine creation, or to His creation by causes not subject to the authority of human capabilities. And within the law of uniformity, people carry out their works, industries, and all affairs of their lives, believing that causes will lead to their known results, and when a defect occurs in the result, man returns and examines himself about the cause, and reconsiders his work to discover the positions of error in the cause he undertook. And under the dominance of the laws of causality and uniformity, scientific research was able to rely on incomplete induction in extracting universal rules and general natural laws, but error in scientific research may come from identifying the cause or from identifying the 'illa; the cause or 'illa may be other than what appeared to the researcher, and the researcher's error comes from his observation of an element accompanying the real cause, so he judges it to be the cause, or from his observation of some elements of the cause so he judges them to be the entire cause, and so on to several factors that make him err in the generalizing judgment he issues. And not all inductive judgments rely on the laws of causality and uniformity; some judgments rely only on the law of accidental uniformity, without this uniformity being due to a cause that explains the occurrence of the uniform cases.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents an epistemological justification for the use of incomplete induction (al-istiqra' al-naqis) in the empirical sciences. It first establishes that incomplete induction is the only path to knowledge in these fields unless one has access to stronger methods, such as definitive religious data. The logical foundation for relying on incomplete induction is the observation of two universal laws governing natural phenomena: the law of causality (qanun al-'illiyya/al-sababiyya) and the law of uniformity (qanun al-ithtiraad). The author explains the law of causality as derived from countless human experiences, showing that every event has a cause. Examples include cloud formation, rain, plant growth, explosions, and thermometer expansion. Although this law is based on incomplete induction, it has never been violated, making it a universally accepted cosmic law. Moreover, the connection between an occurrence and its cause is a purely rational principle, beyond doubt. The law of uniformity states that similar causes produce similar effects. Examples given: cutting the throat causes death; eating leads to satiety; fire consumes living beings. When exceptions occur, they are attributed to an intervening cause (e.g., a pathological condition or a miraculous intervention), and miracles are seen as direct divine creation. People rely on this law in daily life and when errors occur, they investigate the cause. Under these two laws, scientific research uses incomplete induction to derive general rules, but errors can arise from misidentifying the cause—either by mistaking an accompanying factor for the real cause or by taking only a part of the cause for the whole. Finally, the author notes that not all inductive judgments depend on causality; some rely solely on accidental uniformity without a causal basis. The passage uses natural examples from the world, but the logical structure is universal.

Key Terms
الاستقراء الناقص
قانون العلية
قانون الاطراد
السببية

Key Takeaway

Induction and Analogy
intermediate

Errors in Inductive Reasoning and Accidental Uniformity
Discusses sources of error in inductive reasoning and the concept of accidental uniformity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify two sources of error in inductive reasoning as described in the passage: misidentification of cause and reliance on accidental uniformity.

Core Idea
Inductive reasoning can fail when the true cause is misidentified or when uniformity is accidental rather than causal.

While the earlier part of the passage builds confidence in incomplete induction, the latter part introduces important caveats. The author warns that scientific research can err in two main ways: first, by mistaking a mere accompanying factor for the true cause, or by taking only part of the cause as the whole; second, by relying on uniformity that is not rooted in causality (accidental uniformity). These limitations remind us that the inductive method is fallible and requires careful analysis.

Guided Path
1
Locate the error sources in the passage
Find the sentences starting with 'but error in scientific research may come from identifying the cause...' and read until 'several factors that make him err in the generalizing judgment he issues.'
2
Analyze the first error: mistaking an accompanying element for the cause
The author says the researcher may observe an element that accompanies the real cause and mistakenly judge it to be the cause itself. Think of an example: a patient recovers after taking a certain herb, but the recovery might be due to rest, not the herb.
3
Analyze the second error: taking part of the cause for the whole
The researcher may observe only some factors of the true cause and think they are the whole cause. For instance, attributing plant growth solely to watering while ignoring soil quality and sunlight.
4
Understand accidental uniformity
Read the last sentence: 'some judgments rely only on the law of accidental uniformity, without this uniformity being due to a cause.' This means a pattern may occur regularly but without a causal connection, e.g., always wearing a certain shirt when it rains—but the shirt does not cause rain.
5
Reflect on implications
How do these errors affect the reliability of inductive conclusions? They show that induction, while powerful, requires careful reasoning to ensure the observed regularity is causal and not accidental or misattributed.

مرضي أفسد حالته الطبيعية. وكلما قذف حيٌّ في النار أكلته النار فمات ضمن قانون اطراد الآثار السببية التي تحدثها النار، وحينما لا تأكله النار وينجو منها في حالة من الحالات، فإننا نحيل الأمر إلى وجود سبب آخر عطل تأثير النار، ولا بد أن يكون هذا السبب الآخر مبرداً لحرارة النار أو واقياً للجسم من حرها، على أن الأمر لا يخلو من سبب، والمعجزات ترجع إلى سبب الخلق الرباني المباشر، أو إلى خلقه بأسباب غير خاضعة لسلطة القدرات الإنسانية. وضمن قانون الاطراد يقوم الناس بأعمالهم وصناعاتهم وكل شؤون حياتهم، وهم يعتقدون أن الأسباب ستؤدي إلى النتائج المعروفة لها، وحين يحدث خلل في النتيجة يرجع الإنسان فيحاسب نفسه عن السبب، ويعيد النظر في عمله ليكتشف مواقع الخطأ في السبب الذي قام به. وفي هيمنة قانوني السببية والاطراد، استطاع البحث العلمي أن يعتمد على الاستقراء الناقص في استخراج قواعد كلية وقوانين طبيعية عامة، ولكن قد يأتي خطأ البحث العلمي من تحديد السبب أو من تحديد العلة، فقد يكون السبب أو العلة غير الذي ظهر للباحث، ويأتي خطأ الباحث من مشاهدته لعنصر مرافق للسبب الحقيقي، فيحكم عليه بأنه هو السبب، أو من مشاهدته لبعض عناصر السبب فيحكم عليها بأنها هي السبب كله، وهكذا إلى عدة عوامل تجعله يخطئ في الحكم التعميمي الذي يصدره. وليست كل الأحكام الاستقرائية تستند إلى قانوني العلية والاطراد، فبعض الأحكام تستند إلى قانون الاطراد الاتفاقي فقط، دون أن يرجع هذا الاطراد إلى سبب يعلل به حدوث الحالات المطردة.
The only means of its progress, unless a person has a way stronger than incomplete induction and a more upright methodology, like the correct religious data with definitive indications. And the logical basis that calls for adopting this incomplete induction in the field of natural, social, economic, and other sciences, goes back to the observation of two laws dominant over the phenomena of nature in general: they are: the law of causality (i.e., causation), and the law of uniformity. As for the law of causality (i.e., causation): it is the law derived from man's many experiences and his countless experiences, for these experiences and experiments have revealed to him that every event in the universe and every phenomenon among its phenomena is but an effect of some cause or a cause among causes. Thus clouds form due to evaporation of water on earth, and rain falls due to condensation of vapor particles resulting from the passage of a cold wave at the cloud level. And the tree grows due to the presence of the seed in suitable soil and watering. And an explosion occurs somewhere, so all people are certain that some cause has caused this explosion. And the mercury expands in the thermometer, so we know that the reason for that is the rise in temperature. It is true that this law is derived from incomplete induction, but it has not been violated in any experiment of man, nor in any phenomenon of the universe, which made it one of the universally accepted cosmic laws. Added to that is that the origin of the connection of an occurrence to a preceding cause is a purely rational origin, with no room for doubt. And as for the law of uniformity: it is a law also derived from man's many experiences and his countless experiences, and its summary: that similar causes and 'illas produce similar effects and outcomes. So whenever cutting of the neck occurs in the continuous custom, it causes the death of the neck's owner if he is alive. And whenever a person becomes hungry, eats, and fills his stomach, it usually causes the state of satiety and the absence of feeling the need for food, and in the case of his not feeling satiety despite the much food he ate, we refer the matter to a pathological cause that corrupted his natural state. And whenever a living being is thrown into fire, the fire consumes it and it dies within the law of uniformity of the causal effects that fire produces, and when the fire does not consume it and it escapes from it in some case, we refer the matter to the existence of another cause that neutralized the effect of fire, and that other cause must be a cooler of the fire's heat or a protector of the body from its heat, and the matter is not without a cause, and miracles are referred to the cause of direct divine creation, or to His creation by causes not subject to the authority of human capabilities. And within the law of uniformity, people carry out their works, industries, and all affairs of their lives, believing that causes will lead to their known results, and when a defect occurs in the result, man returns and examines himself about the cause, and reconsiders his work to discover the positions of error in the cause he undertook. And under the dominance of the laws of causality and uniformity, scientific research was able to rely on incomplete induction in extracting universal rules and general natural laws, but error in scientific research may come from identifying the cause or from identifying the 'illa; the cause or 'illa may be other than what appeared to the researcher, and the researcher's error comes from his observation of an element accompanying the real cause, so he judges it to be the cause, or from his observation of some elements of the cause so he judges them to be the entire cause, and so on to several factors that make him err in the generalizing judgment he issues. And not all inductive judgments rely on the laws of causality and uniformity; some judgments rely only on the law of accidental uniformity, without this uniformity being due to a cause that explains the occurrence of the uniform cases.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents an epistemological justification for the use of incomplete induction (al-istiqra' al-naqis) in the empirical sciences. It first establishes that incomplete induction is the only path to knowledge in these fields unless one has access to stronger methods, such as definitive religious data. The logical foundation for relying on incomplete induction is the observation of two universal laws governing natural phenomena: the law of causality (qanun al-'illiyya/al-sababiyya) and the law of uniformity (qanun al-ithtiraad). The author explains the law of causality as derived from countless human experiences, showing that every event has a cause. Examples include cloud formation, rain, plant growth, explosions, and thermometer expansion. Although this law is based on incomplete induction, it has never been violated, making it a universally accepted cosmic law. Moreover, the connection between an occurrence and its cause is a purely rational principle, beyond doubt. The law of uniformity states that similar causes produce similar effects. Examples given: cutting the throat causes death; eating leads to satiety; fire consumes living beings. When exceptions occur, they are attributed to an intervening cause (e.g., a pathological condition or a miraculous intervention), and miracles are seen as direct divine creation. People rely on this law in daily life and when errors occur, they investigate the cause. Under these two laws, scientific research uses incomplete induction to derive general rules, but errors can arise from misidentifying the cause—either by mistaking an accompanying factor for the real cause or by taking only a part of the cause for the whole. Finally, the author notes that not all inductive judgments depend on causality; some rely solely on accidental uniformity without a causal basis. The passage uses natural examples from the world, but the logical structure is universal.

Key Terms
الاستقراء الناقص
قانون العلية
قانون الاطراد
الاطراد الاتفاقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one way inductive reasoning can go wrong?
A) The law of causality is always violated.
B) The researcher may mistake an accompanying element for the real cause.
C) Uniformity always implies causation.
D) Incomplete induction is never valid.

Key Takeaway
Inductive reasoning is powerful but prone to errors from cause misidentification and reliance on accidental uniformity.

Induction and Analogy
intermediate
7 min
Two Types of Induction: Causal vs. Uniform
Distinguishes causal and uniform induction with biological examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between causal induction (al-istiqrā' al-ta'līlī) and uniform induction (al-istiqrā' al-iḥṣā'ī) using the criteria of presence or absence of causal explanation.

Core Idea
Causality makes induction certain; mere constancy requires stronger evidence.

The source passage explains that not all regularities are causal. Some are mere accidental uniformity, like mammals having ears or horned animals being cloven-hoofed. Recognizing the difference is crucial for sound reasoning in Islamic sciences, especially when deriving legal rulings or interpreting natural patterns.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Find where the author defines 'causal induction' and 'statistical induction'. Note the characteristics: one involves cause-effect, the other mere constant conjunction.
2
Examine the biological examples
The author gives examples like 'Every mammal has ears' and 'Every animal with horns has a cloven hoof.' Determine whether each example implies causality or just constancy. The author says there is no causal link; it's accidental.
3
Understand the epistemological difference
The author states that when causality is absent, we need a stronger induction (near-perfect) to generalize. When causality is present, the judgment is more complete.
4
Recognize real-world application
Causal induction is used for scientific theories; statistical induction for classifications. In Islamic sciences, causal induction is used when identifying 'illah in usul al-fiqh, while statistical induction is used for authentication of hadith by checking constancy of narrators.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثال ذلك كثير من صفات الحيوان والنبات والجماد المتلازمة تلازماً مطرداً، مع أنه ليس بينها اتصال سببي ظاهر، وإنما وجدت مطردة بشكل اتفاقي .
كل حيوان ذي ثدي له أذن .
كل حيوان ذي قرن مجتر .
كل حيوان ذي قرن مشقوق الظلف .
فاطراد اجتماع الصفتين في هذه الأمثلة ونظائرها، لم يقترن به كون إحدى الصفتين علة للأخرى أو سبباً لها، ولكن اتفق أن كان اجتماع الصفتين أمراً مطرداً، وبناءً على ذلك استطعنا أن نصدر تعميماً بالاطراد مبنياً على الاستقراء الناقص، دون التعرض إلى السببية، ولكن حاجتنا هنا إلى استقراء شبيه بالتام أكثر من حاجتنا إليه في الاطراد المقترن بالعلّة .
وحين يلتقي التعليل والاطراد معاً، يكون الحكم أتم وأوفى، وأنفع في مجال العلوم، وهو الذي يُصْدِر العلماء على أساسه نظرياتهم الطبيعية .
ويطلق على الاستقراء الذي أفاد اطراداً دون تعليل اسم الاستقراء الإحصائي، ويستعمل هذا الاستقراء عادة في الإحصاءات والتصنيفات وما أشبهها .
دلالة القرآن على قانوني العلية والاطراد :
وقد دلنا القرآن على قانوني العلية (السببية) والاطراد في نصوص كثيرة، منها قول الله عزّ وجلّ في سورة (البقرة ٢) :
﴿ وَقَالَ الَّذِينَ لَا يَعْلَمُونَ لَوْلَا يُكَلِّمُنَا اللَّهُ أَوْ تَأْتِينَا ءَايَةٌ كَذَلِكَ قَالَ الَّذِينَ مِن قَبْلِهِم مِّثْلَ قَوْلِهِمْ تَشَبَهَتْ قُلُوبُهُمْ قَدْ بَيَّنَا الْآيَاتِ لِقَوْمٍ يُوقِنُونَ ﴾ .
• فأبانت هذه الآية أن سبب قول الكافرين المتحدث عنهم : «لولا يكلمنا الله أو تأتينا آية» أنّهم لا يعلمون، وأنّ في قلوبهم شيئاً هو من عوامل جحود الحق. فهذا تنبيه على قانون العلية (أي: السببية).
. وأبانت أيضاً أنّ الذين من قبلهم من الكفرة قالوا مثل قولهم، لأن قلوبهم مشابهة لقلوبهم في وجود عوامل جحود الحق فيها. وهذا تنبيه على قانون الاطراد.
Examples of that are many characteristics of animals, plants, and minerals that are constantly concomitant without any apparent causal connection between them, rather they are found constantly in an accidental manner.
Every mammal has ears.
Every ruminant has horns.
Every animal with horns has a cloven hoof.
The constant conjunction of two characteristics in these examples and their likes was not accompanied by one characteristic being the cause or reason for the other, but it happened that their conjunction was constant. Based on that, we were able to issue a generalization of constancy based on incomplete induction, without touching upon causality. However, our need here for an induction resembling perfect is greater than our need for it in the constancy accompanied by cause.
When both causation and constancy meet together, the judgment is most complete and most beneficial in the field of sciences, and it is on that basis that scientists produce their natural theories.
The induction that yields constancy without causation is called statistical induction, and this induction is usually used in statistics, classifications, and the like.
The Qur'an's indication of the two laws of causation and constancy:
The Qur'an has indicated to us the two laws of causation (causality) and constancy in many texts, including the saying of Allah Almighty in Surat al-Baqarah (2):
"And those who do not know say, 'Why does Allah not speak to us or come to us a sign?' Thus said those before them like their words; their hearts resemble each other. We have made the signs clear for a people who are certain."
• So this verse explained that the cause of the unbelievers' statement spoken about, 'Why does Allah not speak to us or come to us a sign?' is that they do not know, and that in their hearts there is something that is among the factors of denying the truth. This is an indication of the law of causation (i.e., causality).
And it also explained that those before them from among the disbelievers said similar sayings because their hearts are similar to their hearts in the presence of factors of denying the truth in them. This is an indication of the law of constancy.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage explains two types of induction: one based on causal connection and the other based on mere constant conjunction (uniformity) without causality. The author first gives examples from biology: mammals always have ears, ruminants always have horns, horned animals always have cloven hooves. These are constant conjunctions, but there is no known causal link between the traits; they simply occur together consistently. From such observations, we can make a generalization of constant conjunction based on incomplete induction, but we need a stronger induction (resembling perfect induction) when there is no causal explanation compared to when causality is present. When both causation and constancy are combined, the judgment is most complete and useful for scientific theories. The author then names the non-causal induction as 'statistical induction,' used in statistics and classifications.

Then the author cites Qur'anic evidence for both laws. The verse from Surah al-Baqarah describes disbelievers who demanded signs. The verse says that their saying 'Why does Allah not speak to us...' is caused by their ignorance and the factors of denial in their hearts—this illustrates the law of causation (the cause of their statement is their ignorance). The verse also says that previous disbelievers said the same thing because their hearts resembled each other—this illustrates the law of constancy: when hearts are similar, similar statements occur consistently. Thus, the Qur'an implicitly establishes both causal and uniform relationships.

Key Terms
الاطراد
العلية (السببية)
الاستقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of induction is used when there is no apparent causal link between two phenomena?
A) Causal induction (istiqrā' ta'līlī)
B) Statistical induction (istiqrā' iḥṣā'ī)
C) Perfect induction
D) Analogy

Key Takeaway
Causal induction provides explanatory power and greater certainty, while uniform induction merely notes constant conjunction and requires stronger evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
From the science of Tafsir: The verse 'And from the fruits of the date-palm and the grape-vine, you take therein intoxicant and good provision' (Quran 16:67) lists both intoxicant and good provision. Could we induce that all fermented drinks are intoxicants using causal induction? Why or why not?

Quranic Evidence for Causality and Constancy
Quranic evidence for laws of causality and constancy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how the Quran implicitly supports the principles of causality (al-`illiyya) and constancy (al-ittirād) in reasoning.

Core Idea
The Quran establishes both causal explanation and uniform patterns as valid bases for knowledge.

The source passage uses a verse from Surat al-Baqarah to demonstrate that the Quran points to both causality and constancy. The verse describes disbelievers who demanded signs: their statement is caused by their ignorance, and their hearts resemble previous disbelievers, showing a constant pattern. Understanding this helps us see that the Quran endorses inductive reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the verse
Read the verse (2:118) and the two indications the author extracts: causal (the cause of their saying is ignorance) and constancy (similar hearts produce similar statements).
2
Recognize the causal law
The cause of the disbelievers' question is their ignorance and denial. This is a causal relationship: ignorance -> demand for signs.
3
Recognize the constancy law
The fact that previous disbelievers said exactly the same thing shows a constant pattern: when hearts are similar, statements are similar. This is an example of ittirād (uniformity) without a causal link stated explicitly.
4
Apply to inductive reasoning
From this verse, we can derive two inductive principles: (1) If ignorance causes certain statements, we can generalize that similar causes lead to similar effects (causal induction). (2) If hearts resemble each other, we can expect similar behavior (uniform induction). Both are used in Islamic interpretation.

Induction and Analogy
beginner
5 min
The Two Laws of Induction: Causality and Uniformity
Author explains two fundamental laws of induction: causality and uniformity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the law of causality and the law of uniformity using Quranic examples.

Core Idea
Induction rests on two pillars: every effect has a cause, and similar causes produce similar effects.

This passage from the Islamic logic tradition identifies two foundational principles that make inductive reasoning possible: causality and uniformity. The author derives these principles directly from a Quranic verse, showing that Islamic epistemology grounds logical methods in revelation. Understanding these laws is essential for evaluating arguments by analogy and generalization in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic verse
The passage refers to the disbelievers' demand: 'Why does God not speak to us or bring us a sign?' (Surah Al-Baqarah). Locate this in the Arabic and note the author's interpretation.
The verse shows the disbelievers' ignorance and inner obstruction.
2
Extract the law of causality
The author says their ignorance is caused by 'factors of denying the truth' in their hearts. This illustrates that every effect (their denial) has a cause (those internal factors).
This is the law of causality (قانون العلية): every effect has a cause.
3
Extract the law of uniformity
The author notes that previous disbelievers said similar things because their hearts resembled the later ones. This shows that similar causes (factors of denial) produce similar effects (denial statements).
This is the law of uniformity (قانون الاطراد): similar causes produce similar effects.
4
Explain the role of these laws in induction
Induction relies on observing patterns and generalizing. Causality assures that observed effects are not random, and uniformity assures that what holds in observed cases will hold in unobserved similar cases.
Without uniformity, induction would be impossible; without causality, correlations would be meaningless.

So this is an indication of the law of causality (i.e., causation). Commentary
This passage explains two fundamental laws that underpin inductive reasoning: causality and uniformity. The author cites a Quranic verse (Surah Al-Baqarah, verse 118) where the disbelievers say, 'Why does God not speak to us or bring us a sign?' This shows their ignorance and the presence in their hearts of factors that lead them to reject the truth. The author uses this to illustrate the law of causality—that every effect has a cause, here the cause of their denial being the factors in their hearts. Then he notes that previous disbelievers said similar things, indicating that their hearts are similar, which points to the law of uniformity (tard)—that similar causes produce similar effects across time. This uniformity is essential for induction because it allows us to generalize from observed patterns.

The Three Stages of Inductive Inquiry
Author enumerates the three stages of inductive inquiry and begins explaining the first stage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three stages of inductive inquiry and distinguish between simple and controlled observation.

Core Idea
Inductive inquiry proceeds through stages: observation, hypothesis, and verification.

The path from scattered particulars to a general law is not immediate. As the author explains, inductive research requires methodical stages. This lesson walks you through those stages and clarifies the critical first step: observation.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the necessity of stages
The author states that a general principle cannot be reached at once through complete or incomplete induction; stages are required. Note the emphasis on gradual, scientific steps.
This refutes the notion that induction is a single leap from particulars to universals.
2
List the three stages
Identify the stages: (1) experiment and observation, (2) scientific hypotheses, (3) verifying or weighting hypotheses with evidence. Memorize this sequence.
3
Examine the first stage: observation
Observation is defined as careful contemplation of particulars to identify their phenomena, attributes, characteristics, and distinguishing features. This is the foundational step.
The author uses 'mulāḥaẓa' for observation, emphasizing active scrutiny.
4
Distinguish simple vs. controlled observation
Simple observation uses senses alone without instruments or laboratory conditions. Controlled observation uses instruments and prepared conditions. Note the author's examples.
This distinction shows that induction can be refined through technology, but both are valid starting points.

Stages of Induction. It is not possible for a researcher to arrive at a general universal principle or a general law through complete or incomplete induction all at once. Therefore, he inevitably needs stages through which he moves stage after stage until he reaches his desired goal with correct scientific steps. Third stage: the stage of verifying hypotheses or weighting them with evidence. And each of these stages has steps, conditions, and points of error that should be guarded against.
